The Model of Saturn and the Supposition of Fixed Stars
Explains the model of Saturn and defends the supposition of fixed stars on it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why fixed stars can be supposed on the model of Saturn and why the objection that Saturn would be eclipsed is refuted by the imperceptibility of eclipses.

Core Idea
Fixed stars can be on Saturn's model because their motions match; any eclipses would be imperceptible.

This lesson continues the discussion by exploring a specific model: placing fixed stars on the sphere that models Saturn's motion. The author explains the components of Saturn's model and why the fixed stars can be placed on its outermost sphere. He then addresses an objection that this would cause Saturn to be eclipsed by fixed stars, and responds that such eclipses are imperceptible due to the similarity of the bodies.

Guided Path
1
Understand the model of Saturn
Learn the terms: mumaththil, hamil, mutammim, tadwir. The model of Saturn includes the carrier and complement spheres.
This is technical astronomical terminology.
2
Reason for supposition
Fixed stars can be placed on Saturn's model because its motion matches that of the sphere of fixed stars.
This is based on observed similarity in motion.
3
Consider the objection
If fixed stars are on that sphere, they would be aligned and eclipse Saturn, contradicting observation.
4
See the response
The author denies the eclipse because both bodies appear similar in shape and color, so the eclipse is imperceptible to the senses.
Magnitude differences do not help when they are aligned.

ثم لم لا يجوز أن يكون بعضها أي بعض الثوابت على أفلاك ) تحت الأفلاك السيارة ) فلا يصح ما ذكروه من الترتيب وحكاية الكسف ) أي كسف السيارات للثوابت على ما ذكروه غير مسلم و ( إن سلم ففيما يقع ) من الثوابت ( في مداراتها ) أي محاذياً لمدارات السيارات حتى يتصور كونها كاسفة لها حاجبة لنا عن رؤيتها فيعلم كون السيارات تحتها فكيف السبيل إلى الجزم في غيرها أي في الثوابت القريبة من القطبين، إذ لا هناك كسف فلا يعلم أنها تجت السيارات أو فوقها ولا يمكن التمسك في ذلك باختلاف المنظر وعدمه. أما بالقياس إلى العلوية فظاهر وأما بالقياس إلى غيرها من الثوابت ما ليست مرصودة لصغرها، فلا يعلم أن لها اختلاف منظر أو لا . قوله : ( وحكاية الكسف إلخ ) فإنه إذا كسف القمر الشمس بقدر ما يكون مثل قطر الزهرة أو قطر عطارد لا يظهر كسوفها للأبصار مع أن الكاسف في غاية الإظلام، فكيف والكاسف نير، فالثوابت تضمحل أنوار السيارات . قوله : ( لجواز فرض الثوابت ودوائر البروج على ممثل (حل اعلم أن ممثل زحل هو مجموع المتمم الحاوي لحامل زحل والمتمم المحوي لهذا الحامل أيضاً، وأما حامل زحل فهو الفلك الخارج المركز لتدوير زحل على ما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى، وإنما جاز فرض الكواكب الثابتة على ممثل زحل لأنهم وجدوا حركة ممثل رحل مثل حركة فلك الثوابت، بحيث يتخيل أن هذا الممثل يتحرك بحركة فلك الثوابت، وسيجيء تفصيله فإن قيل : يلزم من ذلك الفرض أن ينكسف زحل ببعض الثوابت المسامتة له وبالعكس أيضاً، لكن الحس يكذبه، قلنا: حكاية الكسف ممنوعة كما ذكره إذ الحس لا يقدر أن يميز الكاسف عن المنكسف هناك، فإن الظاهر أن أحد الكوكبين مثل الآخر في الشكل واللون، وإن لم يكن في المقدار، لكن عظم المقدار وصغره لا يفيد في تميز أحدهما عن الآخر في الحس عند كونهما متحاذيين في الحس أيضاً.
Then, why is it not permissible for some of the fixed stars—i.e., some of them—to be on spheres beneath the planetary spheres? So what they mentioned of the arrangement and the report of eclipses (kusuf)—i.e., the eclipses of the planets by the fixed stars as they mentioned—is not granted, and if it is granted, then only for those fixed stars that fall in their orbits (i.e., aligned with planetary orbits) so that they can be conceived as eclipsing them and hiding them from us, thus knowing that the planets are beneath them. So how can one be certain regarding others, i.e., the fixed stars near the poles? For there is no eclipse there, so it is not known whether they are below or above the planets, nor can one rely on the existence or absence of parallax (ikhtilaf al-manzar). As for comparison to the superior planets, it is obvious; as for comparison to other fixed stars that are not observed due to their smallness, one does not know whether they have parallax or not. His saying: 'the report of eclipses, etc.'—for if the moon eclipses the sun by an amount equal to the diameter of Venus or Mercury, its eclipse does not appear to the eyes, even though the eclipsing body (the moon) is in extreme darkness; how then when the eclipsing body is luminous? So the fixed stars diminish the lights of the planets. His saying: 'regarding the permissibility of supposing the fixed stars and zodiacal circles on the model of Saturn'—know that the model of Saturn (mumaththil Zuhal) is the sum of the complement (mutammim) containing the carrier (hamil) of Saturn and the complement contained for this carrier also. As for the carrier of Saturn, it is the eccentric sphere (al-falak al-kharij al-markaz) for the epicycle (tadwir) of Saturn, as will come if God wills. It is permissible to suppose the fixed stars on the model of Saturn because they found the motion of the model of Saturn similar to the motion of the sphere of fixed stars, such that one imagines this model moves with the motion of the sphere of fixed stars, and details will come. If it is said: this supposition entails that Saturn would be eclipsed by some fixed stars aligned with it and vice versa, but observation denies it; we say: the report of eclipses is denied as he mentioned, because the senses cannot distinguish the eclipsing from the eclipsed there; for it appears that one of the two planets is like the other in shape and color, even if not in magnitude, but largeness and smallness of magnitude do not help distinguish one from the other by sense when they are aligned in sense as well.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage continues the objection-answer sequence. The objector asks: why cannot some fixed stars be below planetary spheres, disproving the claimed order? The author replies that the evidence from eclipses (where a planet hides a star) is unreliable because eclipses are only observable for stars in the planetary orbits, not for those near the poles. Moreover, even for eclipses, they are imperceptible: if the moon eclipses the sun by the diameter of Venus or Mercury, it is not seen, let alone when the eclipsing body is bright. So fixed stars actually diminish the planets' light. The marginal note about the model of Saturn explains that fixed stars can be placed on Saturn's model because their motions match. An objection that this would cause Saturn to be eclipsed by fixed stars is answered: such eclipses are imperceptible because the senses cannot distinguish the eclipsing from the eclipsed when they are similar in appearance.

تدوير

Misconception: If fixed stars are on Saturn's model, we would observe Saturn being eclipsed by them.
Correction: Such eclipses are imperceptible because the planets look similar to the eyes when aligned.

Checkpoint
Why does the author think the supposition of fixed stars on Saturn's model does not lead to observable eclipses?
A) Because Saturn never aligns with fixed stars.
B) Because the senses cannot distinguish the eclipsing from the eclipsed when they appear similar.
C) Because fixed stars are too far away.
D) Because Saturn is not large enough.

Key Takeaway
The supposition of fixed stars on Saturn's model is permissible, and any potential eclipses would be imperceptible due to their visual similarity.

Real Directions: Above and Below Necessitate a Spherical Body
Concludes that only above and below are real directions, necessitating a spherical body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why above and below are considered the only real directions in kalam cosmology and how this logically necessitates a spherical body.

Core Idea
Above and below are not relative; they define the cosmos as spherical.

In this passage, the author distinguishes between relative directions (like left/right) that change with perspective and the fixed directions of above and below. These unchanging directions require a single defining body, which must be spherical to account for nearness and distance. This conclusion is foundational to classical kalam cosmology and its understanding of the universe's shape.

Guided Path
1
Understand relative vs. real directions
Read the opening where the author states that most directions are relative (i'tibariyya) and changeable, except above and below which are real and unchanging.
Consider why these two are special—they do not depend on the observer's position.
2
Grasp the logical necessity for a defining body
Since above and below are fixed, there must be a body that defines them and determines their positions.
This body must be finite and have a center and periphery.
3
Appreciate why the body must be spherical
The passage argues that the body must be spherical so that nearness is defined by its circumference (above) and distance by its center (below). The commentary adds that the body must be hollow for this distinction to make sense.
If the body were solid, the center would not define a direction clearly.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this reasoning leads to a geocentric spherical universe, a key feature of classical kalam cosmology.
This is a precursor to theological arguments about the existence of a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وقد علمت ) في مباحث الاعتمادات ) أن الجهات على كثرتها اعتبارية ( متبدلة بحسب الأحوال المتغيرة، فلا تدخل تحت الضبط ( ما عدا العلو والسفل، فإنهما جهتان حقيقيتان ) لا تتبدلان أصلا، وإحداهما في غاية البعد عن الأخرى ) فإذن فلا بد من جسم يحددهما ) ويعين وضعهما ( ويكون ) ذلك الجسم المحدد ( كروياً ليتحدد القرب بمحيطه وهو العلو و)

قوله: (مختلفة الحقائق قائمة بأجسام متناهية ) زاد هذين القيدين لأن الدليل الذي ذكره الملاء المتشابه، إنما يبطل عدم كونها مختلفة الحقائق والدليل الذي زاده الشارح رحمه الله تعالى . أعني قوله : وكذلك الحدود المفروضة إلخ، إنما يبطل عدم قيامه بالأجسام المتناهية، لكن لا خفاء في أن المدعى فيما سبق كونها حدوداً ،وأطرافاً لا كونها مختلفة الحقائق، ثم إن كونهما مختلفة الحقائق غير مطلوبة في هذا المقام، وإن كانت كذلك في الواقع فتدبر، فإنه لم يظهر لي حكمة ما قال الشارح أو الموهوم إلخ، بعد ثبوت أن الجهة موجودة. قوله: (وهو الجسم الغير (المتناهي إن تعرضوا هذه للدلالة على أن إثبات المحدد لا يتوقف على إثبات تناهي الأبعاد. قوله : (إذ لا يكون أحد جزأيه إلخ هذا إنما يدل على عدم تعدد الجهات الحقيقية في الملاء المتشابه، والمطلوب عدم تحدد الجهات فيه مطلقاً، فالأولى أن يقال: إذ لا تميز فيه فلا يتعين فيه جزءان يكون منتهى الإشارة مقصد المتحرك . قوله (الحدود المفروضة لا يخفى أن كونها حدوداً ونهايات ينافي كونها مفروضة في الجسم الغير المتناهي، فلا حاجة إلى إبطاله والحق ما قررنا لك، فتدبر حق التدبر. قوله : ( وإحداهما في غاية البعد لا كل واحد منهما إذ لا يمكن ذلك. قوله: (وهو الجسم الذي لا يتناهى إذ الجسم الذي يكون منتهياً يتصور له نهايات وأطراف مختلفة الماهية كما لا يخفى أو أراد أنه الجسم الذي لا يعتبر تناهيه . قوله : ( ليتحدد القرب بمحيطه وهو العلو إلخ) كون العلو جهة القرب والسفل جهة البعد إما يتصور إذ لم يكن ذلك الجسم الكروي مصمتاً، بل كان مجوفاً وأما إذا كان مصمتاً، فالظاهر
And you have known in the investigations of inclinations that the directions, despite their multiplicity, are relative (i'tibariyya), changing according to changing circumstances, so they cannot be fixed, except above (al-'uluww) and below (as-sifl), for they are two real directions, not changing at all, and one of them is in utmost distance from the other. So then there must be a body that defines them and determines their position, and that determining body must be spherical so that nearness is defined by its circumference, which is above, and...

He says: (Different in reality, subsisting in finite bodies) he added these two restrictions because the proof he mentioned regarding the homogeneous matter (mala' mutashabih) only invalidates their not being different in reality, and the proof added by the commentator—God have mercy on him—i.e., his statement: 'Likewise, the supposed boundaries etc.', only invalidates their not subsisting in finite bodies. But there is no hiding that the claim in what preceded is that they are boundaries and extremities, not that they are different in reality. But then their being different in reality is not sought in this context, even if they are so in reality, so reflect. For it has not become clear to me the wisdom of what the commentator said: 'or the imagined etc.', after it is established that direction exists. He says: (And it is the body that is infinite) if they consider this to indicate that the proof of the determiner does not depend on proof of the finitude of dimensions. He says: (For one of its two parts is not, etc.) This only indicates the non-multiplicity of real directions in homogeneous matter, but what is sought is the non-determination of directions in it absolutely. So it is better to say: For there is no distinction in it, so two parts cannot be determined that could be the terminus of pointing and the goal of the moving thing. He says: (The supposed boundaries) it is not hidden that their being boundaries and limits contradicts their being supposed in the infinite body, so there is no need to invalidate it, and the truth is what we set down for you, so reflect well. He says: (And one of them is in utmost distance) not each one, for that is impossible. He says: (And it is the body that is infinite) for the body that is finite can have boundaries and extremities different in reality, as is not hidden, or he meant that it is the body whose finitude is not considered. He says: (So that nearness is defined by its circumference, which is above, etc.) The fact that above is the direction of nearness and below is the direction of distance cannot be imagined unless that spherical body is not solid but hollow; but if it is solid, then it is apparent...
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on directions by distinguishing between relative directions (i'tibariyya) and two real directions: above (al-'uluww) and below (as-sifl). These two are real because they do not change; one is the direction of nearness and the other of distance. This necessitates a body that defines them, and that body must be spherical so that the circumference defines nearness (above). The commentator adds that this spherical body cannot be solid but must be hollow for the distinction to make sense. The commentary also clarifies that the proof about homogeneous matter only invalidates directions not being different in reality, but the main point is that directions are boundaries.

The passage says above and below are 'in utmost distance from each other'. Yet you can only approach one or the other. What current habit or decision is pulling you downward, away from your ultimate purpose? Will you turn toward the 'above' today, even if it means leaving behind what feels comfortable?

Why the Determiner of Up and Down Must Be a Single Sphere
The author proves the necessity of a single spherical body to fix the directions of up and down.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the body that determines the directions of up and down must be a single sphere, and how non-spherical or multiple bodies fail to define these directions uniquely.

Core Idea
Only a single sphere can define both up and down uniquely.

This passage presents a key argument in classical cosmology: the need for a single spherical body to establish the absolute directions of up and down. It shows that without such a body, these directions remain indefinite. As you read, follow the logic step by step to see why the shape and unity of this body are essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Get familiar with the Arabic and English text. Notice the key terms: muḥaddid, kuravī, muḥīṭ, markaz.
2
Identify the two requirements
The passage argues that the determining body must be spherical and single. Find where each requirement is stated and justified.
3
Understand the spherical argument
Why does proximity correspond to the surface and distance to the center? How does a sphere guarantee that the center is the farthest point?
4
Analyze the unity argument
Why would multiple bodies make one superfluous? How does the outermost body suffice?
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with the necessity of a single sphere. Summarize the reasoning in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذن فلا بد من جسم يحددهما ويعين وضعهما، ويكون ذلك الجسم المحدد كروياً ليتحدد القرب بمحيطه وهو العلو ويتحدد البعد بمركزه وهو السفل لأن المركز هو أبعد نقطة عن المحيط بحيث يستحيل أن يفرض في داخله ما هو أبعد منها لأن غير الكروي من الأجسام لا يحدد إلا القرب منه وأما البعد منه فغير محدود لا به وهو ظاهر ولا بغيره من أجسام أخر، إذ يمكن فرضه بحيث يكون البعد أكثر، فلا ينضبط بهما جهتان إحداهما في غاية البعد عن الأخرى، ويكون ذلك الجسم المحدد الكروي واحداً وإلا فإما أن يحيط بعضها ببعض فيكون المحيط هو النهاية الحقيقية التي تنتهي الإشارات الحسية بسطحه الأعلى وقد يكون هو وحده كافياً لتحدد الجهتين به باعتبار مركزه ومحيطه فيكون المحاط حينئذ حشواً لا مدخل له فى تحديد الجهة أصلاً
Therefore, there must be a body that determines them (the two directions) and fixes their position, and that determining body must be spherical, so that proximity is determined by its circumference, which is up, and distance is determined by its center, which is down, because the center is the farthest point from the circumference such that it is impossible to assume within it anything farther than it. For non-spherical bodies determine only proximity to them, but distance from them is not determined — neither by it, which is obvious, nor by other bodies, since it is possible to assume that the distance is greater. Thus two directions one of which is at the extreme distance from the other cannot be fixed by them. And that determining spherical body must be one; otherwise, if some of them surround others, then the surrounding one is the real terminus to which sense-references end at its upper surface, and it alone suffices to determine the two directions by its center and circumference, making that which is surrounded superfluous with no role in determining direction at all.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the body which defines up and down must be spherical and singular, and it explains the reasoning. It begins by concluding that a body is necessary to determine the two real directions (up and down). This body must be spherical so that proximity (up) is defined by its surface circumference and distance (down) by its center, which is the farthest point from the surface. Non-spherical bodies can only define proximity, not distance, because distance from them is indefinite. Additionally, the determining body must be one; if there were multiple, the outermost would be sufficient, rendering the inner ones superfluous.

حشو (ḥashw)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a flat or multi-layered body could define both up and down.
Correction: The passage shows only a single sphere achieves a unique definition of both proximity (up) and distance (down). Non-spherical bodies cannot fix distance, and multiple bodies make inner ones superfluous.

Why the Direction-Defining Body Must Be Spherical
Proof that only a sphere can define opposite directions via its center and periphery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that only a spherical body has a unique farthest interior point (the center), which is necessary to define two opposite directions like up and down.

Core Idea
Only a sphere has a unique farthest interior point—the center.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif argues that to define two opposite directions (like up and down), we need a body with a unique farthest point. Non-spherical bodies cannot provide that, so the defining body must be spherical. Let's follow the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once (first part up to 'فلا ينضبط بهما جهتان') and its translation. Notice the key terms: مركز (center) and محيط (periphery).
The text uses parentheses; these likely indicate the base matn being glossed by commentary.
2
Identify the key claim about the center
The passage states that distance is determined by its center, and the center is the farthest point from the periphery. Ask yourself why this is true only for spheres.
For a sphere, the center is equidistant from all points on the surface and the farthest interior point.
3
Understand why non-spherical bodies fail
Non-spherical bodies can define nearness (proximity) but not farness, because you can always imagine a point farther away inside them. So they cannot establish a unique 'farthest' point.
4
Connect to the requirement for directions
True opposite directions require one direction to be at the utmost distance from the other. Without a unique farthest point, such a pair cannot be defined. Only a sphere provides this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتحدد ( البعد بمركزه وهو السفل لأن المركز هو أبعد نقطة عن المحيط بحيث يستحيل أن يفرض في داخله ما هو أبعد منها ) لأن غير الكروي ) من الأجسام (لا يحدد إلا القرب منه وأما البعد منه فغير محدود ) لا به وهو ظاهر ولا بغيره من أجسام أخر، إذ يمكن فرضه بحيث يكون البعد أكثر ، فلا ينضبط بهما جهتان إحداهما في غاية البعد عن الأخرى، ويكون ذلك الجسم المحدد الكروي ( واحداً وإلا فإما أن يحيط بعضها ببعض فيكون المحيط هو (النهاية الحقيقية التي تنتهي الإشارات الحسية بسطحه الأعلى ( وقد يكون ) هو وحده ( كافياً لتحدد الجهتين به ) باعتبار مركزه ومحيطه فيكون المحاط حينئذ حشواً لا مدخل له فى تحديد الجهة أصلاً، فظهر
Distance is determined by its center, which is the bottom, because the center is the farthest point from the periphery such that it is impossible to suppose within it a point farther than it. Because non-spherical bodies determine only nearness to them, but farness from them is unlimited—not by them, and that is obvious, nor by other bodies, for it can be supposed such that the distance is greater. So two directions, one of which is at the utmost distance from the other, cannot be defined by them. And that spherical determining body must be one; otherwise, either some of them surround others, in which case the surrounding is the true limit at which sensory indications terminate by its upper surface, or it alone suffices to determine the two directions by it, by considering its center and its periphery, so that the surrounded then is a filler having no entry at all in determining the direction. Thus it is evident.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the body which defines the true directions (up and down) must be spherical and unique. It begins by stating that distance is determined by the center (the bottom) because the center is the farthest point from the periphery. Only a sphere has a unique farthest point inside; non-spherical bodies can define only nearness, not farness, because one can always suppose a point farther away. Therefore, such bodies cannot define two opposite directions (one at extreme distance from the other). Next, the passage argues that the determining body must be one: if there were multiple, either they would be nested (the outer one being the true boundary) or separate. If nested, only the outermost defines the directions; if separate, each would only define nearness relative to itself, not a common far point. Hence, a single sphere alone is sufficient, with its center and periphery defining bottom and top respectively. The passage concludes that this is evident.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any shape can define true directions of up and down by its shape.
Correction: Only a sphere has a unique farthest interior point (center) that can serve as the absolute bottom; non-spherical bodies cannot define a farthest point, so they cannot define opposite directions.

Checkpoint
Why can non-spherical bodies not define two opposite directions (one at utmost distance from the other)?
A) Because they are irregular and have no center
B) Because they can define nearness but not a unique farthest point
C) Because they are not made of the same substance as spheres
D) Because they are too small

Key Takeaway
Only a sphere has a unique interior point (the center) that is farthest from all surface points, which is necessary to define true opposite directions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only a sphere can define true opposite directions, what is your life's ultimate reference point that gives you spiritual direction? Are you relying on something that shifts or something absolute?
?
The center is farthest from the periphery—are you willing to be at the 'center' of your faith, distant from the distractions of this world, or do you let the periphery define your priorities?
?
This passage shows that only a body with a unique farthest point can define directions. In your spiritual journey, do you have a unique point of submission that organizes all your priorities, or are you scattered by many near goals? What will you realign today to center your life on Allah?

Why the Spherical Body Must Be Singular
Proof that the spherical body determining directions must be singular, considering nested and separate cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the spherical body that defines directions must be one and not multiple, and why nested spheres still only have one determinant.

Core Idea
If multiple spheres, only the outermost determines true directions; inner spheres are redundant.

After establishing that the body must be spherical, the text now argues that there can only be one such sphere. Multiple spheres would either be nested or separate; either way, only a single sphere actually defines the directions. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'ويكون ذلك الجسم المحدد الكروي واحداً' to the end, along with the translation. Notice the two cases discussed: if some spheres surround others, and if they are separate.
2
Analyze the nested case
If one sphere surrounds another, the outermost sphere's surface is the true limit at which sensory indications end. The inner sphere is a 'filler' and contributes nothing to determining direction.
The key phrase: 'المحيط هو النهاية الحقيقية' (the surrounding is the true limit).
3
Understand the implication for singularity
Even if multiple spheres exist, only the outermost one defines the directions. Thus, a single sphere suffices; additional spheres are unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتحدد ( البعد بمركزه وهو السفل لأن المركز هو أبعد نقطة عن المحيط بحيث يستحيل أن يفرض في داخله ما هو أبعد منها ) لأن غير الكروي ) من الأجسام (لا يحدد إلا القرب منه وأما البعد منه فغير محدود ) لا به وهو ظاهر ولا بغيره من أجسام أخر، إذ يمكن فرضه بحيث يكون البعد أكثر ، فلا ينضبط بهما جهتان إحداهما في غاية البعد عن الأخرى، ويكون ذلك الجسم المحدد الكروي ( واحداً وإلا فإما أن يحيط بعضها ببعض فيكون المحيط هو (النهاية الحقيقية التي تنتهي الإشارات الحسية بسطحه الأعلى ( وقد يكون ) هو وحده ( كافياً لتحدد الجهتين به ) باعتبار مركزه ومحيطه فيكون المحاط حينئذ حشواً لا مدخل له فى تحديد الجهة أصلاً، فظهر
Distance is determined by its center, which is the bottom, because the center is the farthest point from the periphery such that it is impossible to suppose within it a point farther than it. Because non-spherical bodies determine only nearness to them, but farness from them is unlimited—not by them, and that is obvious, nor by other bodies, for it can be supposed such that the distance is greater. So two directions, one of which is at the utmost distance from the other, cannot be defined by them. And that spherical determining body must be one; otherwise, either some of them surround others, in which case the surrounding is the true limit at which sensory indications terminate by its upper surface, or it alone suffices to determine the two directions by it, by considering its center and its periphery, so that the surrounded then is a filler having no entry at all in determining the direction. Thus it is evident.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the body which defines the true directions (up and down) must be spherical and unique. It begins by stating that distance is determined by the center (the bottom) because the center is the farthest point from the periphery. Only a sphere has a unique farthest point inside; non-spherical bodies can define only nearness, not farness, because one can always suppose a point farther away. Therefore, such bodies cannot define two opposite directions (one at extreme distance from the other). Next, the passage argues that the determining body must be one: if there were multiple, either they would be nested (the outer one being the true boundary) or separate. If nested, only the outermost defines the directions; if separate, each would only define nearness relative to itself, not a common far point. Hence, a single sphere alone is sufficient, with its center and periphery defining bottom and top respectively. The passage concludes that this is evident.

Key Terms
جسم كروي
محيط
مركز
حشو
نهاية حقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple spheres could define directions jointly, e.g., each defining its own up and down.
Correction: Only one sphere provides the unique farthest point; nested spheres are redundant, and separate spheres would not share a common farthest point.

Checkpoint
If multiple spheres exist and some are nested inside others, which sphere determines the true directions?
A) The innermost sphere
B) The outermost sphere
C) All spheres together
D) The largest sphere by volume

Key Takeaway
Only one spherical body—the outermost—defines the true directions; any inner spheres are redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual life, are there 'inner spheres'—habits, practices, or distractions—that you treat as essential but are actually filler, preventing you from focusing solely on what truly determines your direction towards Allah? What is one such thing you can remove this week?
?
The passage says that the surrounded sphere has no entry in determining direction. Could it be that some of your pursuits, though good, are actually irrelevant to your ultimate purpose? How would stripping them away change your daily orientation?
?
If only the outermost sphere matters, all your energy should be directed to that. What is your 'outermost sphere'—the one thing that defines your final direction? Are you giving it your primary attention, or are you distracted by filler?

Why the Moon Cannot Define Directions
Refutes the claim that the lunar sphere defines directions and reaffirms the necessity of an all-encompassing sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sphere of the moon cannot determine true directions for sublunary bodies and why an all-encompassing sphere is necessary to define above and below.

Core Idea
Direction requires an all-encompassing sphere, not a partial one.

Sanusi refutes an alternative cosmological view that the lunar sphere determines directions. Through two logical arguments, he shows that such a view leads to contradictions and fails to define farness. He then reaffirms that only a sphere encompassing all bodies can serve as the reference for true directions. This lesson unpacks the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a refutation followed by a reaffirmation of the correct position.
The passage uses parentheses which may indicate interpolation from commentary.
2
Identify the two refutation arguments
The passage begins by stating the corruption of the lunar sphere claim. Then it presents two arguments: first, that if two spheres are not enclosing, directions would exist prior to them; second, that the lunar sphere can only define nearness, not farness. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the correct conclusion
The passage concludes that there must be a single sphere encompassing all bodies, whose upper surface is above and center is below. This resolves the issue of direction determination.
This is what the author set out to prove.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فساد ما قيل من أن فلك القمر يحدد جهات الأجسام القابلة للحركة المستقيمة ) أو لا يحيط ) بعضها ببعض ( بل يكون كل منهما خارجاً واقعاً ( في جهة من الآخر فتكون الجهة متحدة قبلهما حتى يمكن وقوعهما فيها (لا) متحددة (بهما والمفروض خلافه وأيضاً فلا يتحدد بشيء منهما إلا جهة القرب دون البعد كما فإن البعد عن الجسم إذا كان خارجاً عنه، فالبعد عنه إلى أين ( فقد ثبت ) بما قررناه ( وجود كرة بها تتحدد الجهات الحقيقية ( محيطة بالكل) أي بجميع الأجسام ليكون سطحه الأعلى منتهى الإشارات وجهة الفوق ومركزه الذي يتساوى بعده عنه، وتنتهي به الإشارة النازلة عنه جهة التحت ) وهو المطلوب
The corruption of what was said: that the sphere of the moon determines the directions of bodies capable of rectilinear motion. Or they do not surround each other, but rather each lies outside, occurring in a direction from the other, so that the direction would be unified before them until they can occur in it, not determined by them, contrary to what is assumed. Also, nothing from them determines except the direction of nearness, not farness, as when a body is outside it, the distance from it is to where? It has been established by what we have set forth that there exists a sphere by which the true directions are determined, encompassing all bodies, so that its upper surface is the terminus of upward indications and the direction of above, and its center, from which its distance is equal, and at which downward indications terminate, is the direction of below. That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage refutes an alternative theory that the sphere of the moon (the nearest celestial sphere) could determine the directions for bodies that undergo rectilinear motion (sublunary elements). The refutation argues that if there were two such spheres (or bodies) not enclosing each other, each would lie in a direction from the other, implying that directions exist prior to them—contradicting the idea that they determine directions. Moreover, each sphere can only define nearness relative to itself, not a universal farness. For a body outside the moon's sphere, one cannot specify a unique far direction. The passage then reaffirms the previously established proof: there must be a single sphere encompassing all bodies, whose upper surface defines the direction 'above' and whose center defines 'below'. This is the correct conclusion.

Key Terms
فلك القمر
جهات حقيقية
فوق
تحت
كرة محيطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sphere of the moon can define true above and below for sublunary bodies.
Correction: The lunar sphere only defines nearness relative to itself, not a universal farness. Only an all-encompassing sphere with a unique center can define true directions.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage reject the idea that the moon's sphere determines directions?
A) Because the moon's sphere is too small
B) Because it cannot define the direction of farness for bodies outside it
C) Because it rotates and changes orientation
D) Because it is not spherical

Key Takeaway
Only an all-encompassing sphere with a unique center can define true above and below; the moon's sphere is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, what 'lunar spheres'—partial criteria or limited perspectives—are you relying on to define your direction? How does this lesson compel you to seek the All-Encompassing as your ultimate reference?
?
Do you live as if there is an absolute above and below, a real purpose and foundation? If you truly believed the universe has an ultimate center and boundary, what would you change about your priorities today?
?
This lesson argues that only a sphere surrounding all can give meaningful direction. What is the 'all-encompassing sphere' in your spiritual life? Are you willing to let go of partial guides and submit to the One who encompasses everything?

The Determining Sphere Is Simple, Not Composite
Proof that the sphere defining directions must be simple and indivisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the celestial sphere that determines the true directions (al-muhaddid) must be simple and indivisible, based on a conditional proof from compositeness leading to dissolution.

Core Idea
A composite sphere could disintegrate—but the cosmic sphere cannot.

In this brief but crucial argument, the author establishes that the sphere responsible for defining the six directions must be a single, simple body. If it were made of multiple parts, those parts could separate—but the sphere is permanent and defines fixed directions. This passage presents a logical proof by contradiction: assuming compositeness implies possible dissolution, which is false, so the assumption is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the compact logical structure: a conditional statement with its justification.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key terms
Notice the terms 'بسيط' (simple), 'مركب' (composite), and 'انحلال' (dissolution). Understand their meanings in this context.
3
Trace the logical argument
The argument is: If composite → dissolution possible. But dissolution is impossible. Therefore composite is false; hence the sphere is simple. Identify each part.
This is a proof by contradiction (implication and counter-implication).
4
Reflect on the implication
The author explains why compositeness would allow dissolution: each part would have different adjacencies, leading to possible separation. A simple body has uniform adjacencies.
This is the 'الملزومية' part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم له ) أي للمحدد ) أحكام منها أنه بسيط لا مركب من بسائط متعددة ) وإلا جاز انحلاله واللازم باطل) فالملزوم مثله ( أما الملزومية فلان ( المحدد إذا كان مركباً من بسائط متعددة كان كل واحد من أجزائه ملاقياً بأحد جانبيه شيئاً غير ما يلاقيه بجانبه الآخر، ولا شك أن البسيط يمكنه أن يلاقي بأحد طرفيه ما يلاقيه بالآخر لتساويهما) أي تساوي
Then it—i.e., the determiner—has rulings, among them: that it is simple, not composed of multiple simples. Otherwise, its dissolution would be possible, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. As for the implication: because if the determiner were composed of multiple simples, then each of its parts would be adjacent on one of its sides to something other than what it is adjacent to on its other side, and there is no doubt that the simple can be adjacent on one of its ends to what it is adjacent to on the other, because of their equality—i.e., equality.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that the sphere that determines the true directions must be simple (بسيط) and not composed of multiple simple parts (بسائط متعددة). The argument is structured as a conditional proof: if it were composite, then it could disintegrate (انحلال); but disintegration is impossible for this sphere (presumably because it is the outermost sphere or because it is necessary for defining fixed directions). The implication (الملزومية) is explained: if the determiner were composite, each part would have a different adjacency on its two sides (since it would have distinct parts touching different things). However, a simple body can have the same adjacency on both sides because its two ends are equal in nature. The passage breaks off at 'equality', but the reasoning is clear: a composite would have asymmetry leading to potential separation, which is not possible for the cosmic sphere.

Key Terms
بسيط
مركب
انحلال
ملاقاة
تساوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the sphere could be composite like a layered sphere, without affecting its function.
Correction: Compositeness would imply the possibility of dissolution, which contradicts the permanence and fixed directional role of the sphere.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given for the determining sphere being simple rather than composite?
A) Because compositeness would allow disassembly, which is impossible for the cosmic sphere
B) Because a composite sphere would be heavier
C) Because a simple sphere is more beautiful
D) Because the sphere must be made of a single element

Key Takeaway
The determining sphere must be simple because compositeness would imply possible dissolution, which is impossible for the fixed cosmic order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The sphere’s simplicity ensures the stability of directions and the order of the universe. How often do you pause to reflect on the stability of the world around you? Does this awareness move you to gratitude toward the Creator who established it? What would change in your daily life if you actively remembered that this order depends on a simple, unchanging foundation?
?
The argument says that compositeness implies possible dissolution. Consider the things in your life that you rely on—your health, relationships, career. Are they composite, made of parts that could fall apart? How does that fragility affect your peace of mind? What would it mean for you to anchor your ultimate hope in that which is truly simple and cannot dissolve—the One who is simple in the absolute sense?

Contact of Similar Bodies Requires Dissolution and Rectilinear Motion
Al-Jurjani argues for the necessity of dissolution and rectilinear motion in contact of similar bodies, with an objection and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the contact of two similar simple bodies logically necessitates dissolution and rectilinear motion, and how an objection based on circular motion is refuted.

Core Idea
Contact of similar bodies forces separation and straight-line movement; only then can another part touch the same object.

Al-Jurjani begins a detailed argument about the conditions necessary for two similar simple bodies to touch the same object. He claims that such contact requires the first body to dissolve, which in turn demands rectilinear motion. An objection is raised that circular motion might suffice, but the author argues that rectilinear motion alone can bring parts from one determined direction to another.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to follow the argument line.
Notice the use of parentheses for explanatory phrases.
2
Identify the main claim and proof
Find the statement that contact requires dissolution and the reason why dissolution requires rectilinear motion.
The proof links dissolution to separation of parts via straight-line motion.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Understand the objection that circular motion could achieve contact without dissolution, and see how the response argues that rectilinear motion requires a predetermined direction.
The response establishes that direction must be set before the motion begins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم الطرفين في الماهية فإذا لاقى أحدهما شيئاً جاز أن يلاقيه الآخر، وذلك إنما يتصور بالانحلال . ( وأما بطلان اللازم فلأن ذلك ) أي الانحلال ) لا يكون إلا بالحركة المستقيمة وتباعد بعض الأجزاء عن بعض . وقد يقال : جاز أن تكون الملاقاة بالحركة المستديرة، فلا يلزم الانحلال المستلزم للحركة المستقيمة ( وهي ) أعني الحركة المستقيمة ( لا تكون إلا من جهة إلى جهة ) أخرى ( فتكون الجهة متحددة قبله ) أي قبل المحدد حتى يمكن حركة أجزائه إليها ( لا ) متحددة به هذا خلف ومنها ) أي ومن أحكام المحدد ( أنه شفاف) لا لون له ( وكذلك سائر الأفلاك ) شفافة غير ملونة، وذلك ( لأنها لا تحجب الأبصار عن رؤية ما وراءها ) من الكواكب، وكل ملون فإنه يحجب عن ذلك قال الإمام الرازي: لا نسلم أن كل ملون حاجب فإن الماء والزجاج ملونان لأنهما مرئيان ومع ذلك لا يحجبان، فلأن قيل فيهما حجب عن الأبصار الكامل قلنا : وكيف عرفتم أنكم أدركتم هذه الكواكب إدراكاً تاماً ( واعلم أن هذا الذي ذكروه ( لا يتمشى في المحدد، إذ ليس له وراء ) حتى يرى ولا في فلك الثوابت أيضاً، إذ ليس فوقه كوكب مرئي (إلا) أن يقال : لو كان ) المحدد أو فلك الثوابت ) ملوناً لوجب رؤيته، فنقول : ( جاز أن يكون لونه ضعيفاً كلون الزجاج، فلا يرى من بعيد ولئن سلمنا وجوب رؤيته لونه قلنا ) ولم لا يجوز أن تكون هذه
،
قوله : ( وقد يقال إلخ) فيه أن هذا إنما يتصور إذا كان ذلك الجسم والأجزاء كلها كروية الشكل وتركيب الجسم منها بوقوع الفرج بينها ، أما إذا كانت مضلعة الحركة كل واحد منهما، وإن كانت على نفسه يقتضي تبدل أمكنتها صغراً وكبراً ولا بالحركة المستقيمة إلى ذلك . قوله : ( إلا ) من جهة أي من جهة حقيقية إلى جهة حقيقية، لأن المكانين المتباينين في الوضع، إما طبيعيان أو قسريان أو أحدهما قسري والآخر طبيعي، وعلى التقديرين لا بد من وقوعها في الجهة الحقيقية، كما لا يخفى.
يلاقيه راجع إلى البسيط المذكور والضمير المنصوب البارز في قوله : يلاقيه راجع إلى ما، وفي هذا المقام منع وهو أن يقال : الإمكان المذكور وإن كان مسلماً بالنسبة إلى البسائط لكنه ممنوع بالنسبة إلى المجموع المركب من تلك البسائط، فإنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون طبيعة الكل مانعة عن حركة البسائط بوجه ما أصلا.
قوله : ( وقد يقال : جاز أن يكون إلخ ) فيه بحث فإنه لا شك أن بعض الأجزاء البسيطة حينئذ كان قريباً من السطح الأعلى لذلك الفلك المركب، وأن البعض الآخر من تلك الأجزاء كان قريباً من السطح الأسفل، لذلك الفلك أيضاً، وظاهر أن ما بين السطحين المذكورين بون بعيد بین هذا البسيط الأقرب من السطح الأسفل وبين ذلك البسيط الأقرب من السطح الأعلى إلا بالحركة المستقيمة كما لا يخفى .
وأنه لا يتصور الملاقاة
The First Observatory - Chapter Two: Section One: Second Purpose: On the directions of the body. The two extremes in quiddity. So when one of them touches something, it is possible for the other to touch it, and that is only conceivable through dissolution. (As for the falsity of the consequent, it is because that) (i.e., dissolution) occurs only through rectilinear motion and the separation of some parts from others. And it may be said: it is possible for the contact to be through circular motion, so dissolution necessitating rectilinear motion is not required. (And it) (I mean rectilinear motion) (occurs only from one direction to another) direction, so the direction becomes determined before it (i.e., before the determiner [al-muhaddid]) so that the motion of its parts toward it is possible, (not) determined by it; this is a contradiction. And among them (i.e., among the properties of the determiner) is that (it is transparent) having no color, (and likewise the rest of the spheres) are transparent, uncolored, (because they do not block vision from seeing what is beyond them) of the stars, and everything colored blocks that. Imam al-Razi said: We do not grant that everything colored is a barrier, for water and glass are colored because they are visible and yet do not block. For if it is said about them that they block complete vision, we say: How do you know that you have perceived these stars completely? (And know that what they mentioned) does not apply to the determiner (since it has no beyond) to be seen, nor to the sphere of the fixed stars (since there is no visible star above it). (Unless) it is said: if (the determiner or the sphere of the fixed stars) were colored, it would necessarily be seen. Then we say: (It is possible that its color is weak, like the color of glass, so it is not seen from afar. And if we grant the necessity of seeing its color, we say): And why may it not be that these...

His statement: (And it may be said, etc.) In it is that this is only conceivable if that body and its parts are all spherical in shape and the composition of the body from them is with gaps between them. But if they are polygonal, the motion of each one of them, even if in itself requires the change of their places in smallness and largeness, and not by rectilinear motion to that. His statement: (except) from a direction, i.e., from a real direction to a real direction, because the two places differing in position are either natural or constrained, or one constrained and the other natural; in both cases, it must occur in the real direction, as is not hidden.

'He touches' refers to the aforementioned simple body, and the explicit accusative pronoun in his statement 'he touches' refers to 'what'. In this context, there is a prevention, namely that it said: the aforementioned possibility, even if granted with respect to the simples, is prevented with respect to the whole composed of those simples, for why may it not be that the nature of the whole prevents the motion of the simples in any way at all?

His statement: (And it may be said: it is possible that it be, etc.) In it is a discussion, for there is no doubt that some of the simple parts at that time were near the upper surface of that composite sphere, and the other parts were near the lower surface of that sphere as well. It is apparent that the distance between the two mentioned surfaces is a great distance between this simple part nearest to the lower surface and that simple part nearest to the upper surface except by rectilinear motion, as is not hidden.

And that contact is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage begins with the heading for a section on directions of the body and presents an argument about contact of similar entities. The main claim is that if one of two similar simple bodies touches something, the other can also touch it, but this is only conceivable through dissolution (انحلال) of the first body. The argument then proceeds: the falsity of the consequent (i.e., that contact requires dissolution) is because dissolution only occurs through rectilinear motion and separation of parts. An objection is raised: it might be possible for contact to occur through circular motion without dissolution. The answer retorts that rectilinear motion is from one direction to another, so the direction must be determined before the moving body (i.e., the outermost sphere, al-muhaddid) to allow its parts to move toward it; if the direction were determined by the body itself, that would be a contradiction (خلف). The text then begins a new point about transparency: among the properties of the determiner is that it is transparent, as are all spheres, because they do not block vision of stars beyond. An objection from al-Razi is reported: not every colored thing blocks vision, as water and glass are colored yet do not block. The reply is that perhaps they block complete vision, and the opponent cannot prove they perceive the stars completely. The argument then notes that this defense does not apply to the determiner (since it has no beyond) or the sphere of fixed stars (since no visible star is above). Another objection: if they were colored, they would be seen. The answer: their color might be weak like glass, so not seen from afar; even if we grant visibility, why could the color not be something else? The text then transitions to a commentary (قوله) analyzing the initial objection about circular motion. The commentary states that the objection only works if the parts are spherical with gaps; if they are polygonal, the motion would involve change of place in size. It also clarifies that 'direction' means a real direction, and discusses the prevention of the possibility if the whole's nature prevents motion. Another commentary revisits the objection about circular motion, arguing that given the spatial separation of parts, only rectilinear motion can bring them together, so contact via circular motion is inconceivable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Circular motion can achieve contact of similar bodies without dissolution or rectilinear motion.
Correction: The passage shows that rectilinear motion is needed because contact requires parts to move from one determined direction to another, which circular motion cannot accomplish alone.

Checkpoint

A) Circular motion
B) Dissolution and rectilinear motion
C) No motion at all
D) The presence of a medium

Key Takeaway
Contact of similar simple bodies forces dissolution because parts must separate via rectilinear motion toward a predetermined direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Commit to identifying one such area and tracing its reliance on Allah.
?

Transparency of the Spheres and the Determiner
Al-Jurjani argues that the determiner and spheres are transparent, with objections and replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the outermost sphere (al-muhaddid) and all celestial spheres must be transparent, and how objections about color and visibility are answered.

Core Idea
Celestial spheres are transparent because they do not block vision of stars, and their color, if any, would be too weak to see.

After establishing properties of the determiner (al-muhaddid), al-Jurjani argues that it and all spheres are transparent and colorless. He supports this by noting that they do not block our view of stars. Imam al-Razi objects that colored things like water and glass do not block vision. The reply distinguishes between weak and strong colors, and notes that the determiner has no beyond to see.

Guided Path
1
Read the transparency passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the claim and the supporting reason.
The proof is empirical: we see stars through the spheres.
2
Understand the objection by al-Razi
Al-Razi challenges the generalization that all colored things block vision, citing water and glass as counterexamples.
He does not deny that spheres are transparent, but disputes the proof.
3
Analyze the replies
The author responds: water and glass may block complete vision; also, for the determiner and fixed stars sphere, there is no beyond to see, so transparency is not required for vision. A further objection about visibility of a colored sphere is answered by weak color.
The final reply questions the assumption that color must be visible from afar.

The Blueness of the Sky: Real or Imagined?
Al-Jurjani defends the reality of the sky's blueness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the blue color of the sky can be interpreted as a real color of the celestial spheres, not merely an optical illusion caused by transparency.

Core Idea
The sky's blueness may be a real color, not just an imagined one.

This lesson addresses a common objection to the transparency of celestial spheres. Critics argue that the blue sky is simply an illusion due to depth, like sea water. But al-Jurjani offers a counterargument: blueness can be a real quality, and there is no proof it is only imaginary. This opens the possibility that the spheres themselves are blue.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice how the objection and response are structured.
The passage includes parenthetical clarifications.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that the blueness of the sky is an imagined color due to depth, like sea water. Find where this is stated.
It begins with 'لا يقال'.
3
Understand the response
Al-Jurjani responds that blueness can be a real color and there is no proof it is only imaginary. This allows that the sky's color might be real.
Look for 'لأنا نقول'.
4
Consider the implication
If the blueness is real, it could be a property of the spheres themselves. This supports the idea that the spheres are colored bodies.

The First Observatory - Chapter Two: Section One: Second Purpose: On the directions of...

The blue (pure) (visible its color? It is not said: that) (i.e., the color of blueness) (is a thing perceived in the transparent when its depth is far, as in sea water, for it is seen blue, varying in blueness according to the variation of its depth near and far. So the mentioned blueness is an imagined color in the air between heaven and earth, because it is transparent with deep depth). (For we say): (Blueness may be an imagined color as you mentioned, and may also be a real color subsisting in bodies. (As for the proof) existing (that it occurs only by that imaginary way) (i.e., there is no proof for that), so it is possible that the visible blueness is a real color of one of the two spheres. (And among them is that) (I mean the determiner [al-muhaddid]) (is neither heavy nor light, because they) (i.e., lightness and heaviness) (are the principle of ascending and descending inclination) or these two inclinations themselves, according to the difference of interpretations. (And they) validate the motion of their locus (by rectilinearity); so (the existence of heaviness or lightness in the determiner necessitates the permissibility of rectilinear motion upon it, and that necessitates) the determination of the direction before (it, i.e., before it, not by it). And this proof, because it is based on the determination of direction, is specific to the determiner (and does not apply to the remaining spheres). (And the general proof) for all (is that they move circularly by the indication of observations, so in them there is a principle of circular inclination, rather a circular inclination as well, because it is the proximate necessitator of circular motion). So there is no principle of rectilinear motion in them due to their incompatibility. (And the incompatibility may be denied) between the two inclinations, (for they may coexist) in one body, (and by their coexistence) in it (a composite motion occurs, like rolling) in a sphere, (and like the wheel), for it moves rectilinearly and circularly together. (And circular motion is not a diverting force) away from the direction, but rather it does not necessitate orientation toward it. And if the incompatibility between the two inclinations is granted, there is no incompatibility between the two principles nor between one of them and the principle of the other, for the stone thrown upward has in it a principle...

His statement: (For it moves rectilinearly, etc.) It is not hidden that conventional circular motion is conditioned by the moving body not exiting its place, so there is no circular motion in it in that way. By this it is shown that circular motion requires non-orientation toward a direction because it does not necessitate orientation toward it.

His statement: (between the two inclinations) i.e., between the two inclinations, i.e., between the two propulsions.

His statement: (For the stone, etc.) In it is that the intended meaning is that there is no natural rectilinear principle of motion in it, because it is impossible for a single nature, if left to itself and its nature, to necessitate two contradictory things. And if it is not natural, then it is not constrained, because it is established that where there is no nature, there is no constraint, and there is no rectilinear principle of motion in the spheres, neither natural nor constrained.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage begins on page 90 with the word 'Lightness' possibly as a heading, followed by a section header. The discussion continues from the previous page about the transparency of the spheres. It addresses an objection: the blue color seen in the sky might be an imagined color due to depth, like the blue of sea water. The response is that blueness can be a real color subsisting in bodies, and there is no proof that it occurs only by that imaginary way, so it could be a real color of one of the spheres (the determiner or the fixed stars). Then a new argument begins: the determiner (al-muhaddid) is neither heavy nor light because heaviness and lightness are principles of ascending and descending rectilinear motion. If the determiner had such a principle, it would allow rectilinear motion, which would require a determined direction before it, leading to a contradiction. This proof is specific to the determiner. Then a general proof for all spheres is given: they are observed to move circularly, so they have a principle of circular inclination, not rectilinear, due to incompatibility. An objection is raised: the incompatibility may be denied because the two inclinations can coexist, producing a composite motion like rolling of a sphere or a wheel moving both rectilinearly and circularly. The response is that circular motion does not necessitate orientation toward a direction, so it does not conflict. If incompatibility is granted, it does not imply incompatibility between the principles themselves. The commentary clarifies that conventional circular motion does not involve exiting the place, so it does not require orientation. It also explains that the stone thrown upward does not have a natural rectilinear principle because nature cannot necessitate contradictory motions; hence, there is no natural or constrained rectilinear motion in the spheres.

D) The blueness is not discussed.

Key Takeaway
The blue of the sky could be a real color of the celestial spheres, as there is no proof it is merely imagined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the sky, do you assume its blueness is merely an illusion, or do you consider it a real quality of creation? How does Al-Jurjani's argument challenge you to not dismiss what you see as unreal? Will you today pause and reflect on the reality of Allah's signs beyond your assumptions?
?
The objector used a strong analogy (sea water) to prove a point. Are there analogies people use to weaken your faith that you have accepted without questioning? Will you examine these analogies with the same critical eye Al-Jurjani used?
?
Al-Jurjani defends the possibility that the sky's color is real. Does this change how you view the beauty of creation? Are you willing to see the world as genuinely reflecting its Creator, not just as a product of random illusions? Commit to looking at the sky tomorrow with gratitude for its real beauty.

Why Celestial Spheres Have Only Circular Motion
Al-Jurjani argues that the spheres lack rectilinear motion and have only circular inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments that celestial spheres possess only circular motion and that the presence of heaviness/lightness would imply rectilinear motion, which is incompatible with their nature.

Core Idea
Spheres move circularly by nature, not rectilinearly.

This lesson presents the core arguments for why the heavenly spheres cannot have rectilinear motion. The determiner (al-muhaddid) is neither heavy nor light because that would necessitate rectilinear motion and a predetermined direction. Observations confirm circular motion, and any apparent composite motion (like rolling) does not apply to the spheres' technical circular motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about the determiner
The determiner (the outermost sphere) is neither heavy nor light. If it were, it would allow rectilinear motion, which would require a direction determined before it, a contradiction.
This proof is specific to the determiner.
2
Understand the general proof from observation
All spheres are observed to move circularly, so they must have a principle of circular inclination, not rectilinear.
This is based on empirical observations (الأرصاد).
3
Consider the objection of composite motion
An objector might say that a rolling ball or a wheel moves both rectilinearly and circularly, so the two inclinations can coexist. Find this objection.
Look for 'وقد يمنع التنافي'.
4
Grasp the technical definition of circular motion
The response clarifies that conventional circular motion, as defined in physics, does not involve exiting the place and does not necessitate orientation toward a direction. Thus, it does not conflict with rectilinear motion in a composite way.
The commentary explains that the stone thrown upward has only one natural inclination.
5
Conclude that spheres have only circular inclination
Therefore, the spheres have no natural or constrained rectilinear motion; only circular motion.

The First Observatory - Chapter Two: Section One: Second Purpose: On the directions of...

The blue (pure) (visible its color? It is not said: that) (i.e., the color of blueness) (is a thing perceived in the transparent when its depth is far, as in sea water, for it is seen blue, varying in blueness according to the variation of its depth near and far. So the mentioned blueness is an imagined color in the air between heaven and earth, because it is transparent with deep depth). (For we say): (Blueness may be an imagined color as you mentioned, and may also be a real color subsisting in bodies. (As for the proof) existing (that it occurs only by that imaginary way) (i.e., there is no proof for that), so it is possible that the visible blueness is a real color of one of the two spheres. (And among them is that) (I mean the determiner [al-muhaddid]) (is neither heavy nor light, because they) (i.e., lightness and heaviness) (are the principle of ascending and descending inclination) or these two inclinations themselves, according to the difference of interpretations. (And they) validate the motion of their locus (by rectilinearity); so (the existence of heaviness or lightness in the determiner necessitates the permissibility of rectilinear motion upon it, and that necessitates) the determination of the direction before (it, i.e., before it, not by it). And this proof, because it is based on the determination of direction, is specific to the determiner (and does not apply to the remaining spheres). (And the general proof) for all (is that they move circularly by the indication of observations, so in them there is a principle of circular inclination, rather a circular inclination as well, because it is the proximate necessitator of circular motion). So there is no principle of rectilinear motion in them due to their incompatibility. (And the incompatibility may be denied) between the two inclinations, (for they may coexist) in one body, (and by their coexistence) in it (a composite motion occurs, like rolling) in a sphere, (and like the wheel), for it moves rectilinearly and circularly together. (And circular motion is not a diverting force) away from the direction, but rather it does not necessitate orientation toward it. And if the incompatibility between the two inclinations is granted, there is no incompatibility between the two principles nor between one of them and the principle of the other, for the stone thrown upward has in it a principle...

His statement: (For it moves rectilinearly, etc.) It is not hidden that conventional circular motion is conditioned by the moving body not exiting its place, so there is no circular motion in it in that way. By this it is shown that circular motion requires non-orientation toward a direction because it does not necessitate orientation toward it.

His statement: (between the two inclinations) i.e., between the two inclinations, i.e., between the two propulsions.

His statement: (For the stone, etc.) In it is that the intended meaning is that there is no natural rectilinear principle of motion in it, because it is impossible for a single nature, if left to itself and its nature, to necessitate two contradictory things. And if it is not natural, then it is not constrained, because it is established that where there is no nature, there is no constraint, and there is no rectilinear principle of motion in the spheres, neither natural nor constrained.

Commentary
This passage begins on page 90 with the word 'Lightness' possibly as a heading, followed by a section header. The discussion continues from the previous page about the transparency of the spheres. It addresses an objection: the blue color seen in the sky might be an imagined color due to depth, like the blue of sea water. The response is that blueness can be a real color subsisting in bodies, and there is no proof that it occurs only by that imaginary way, so it could be a real color of one of the spheres (the determiner or the fixed stars). Then a new argument begins: the determiner (al-muhaddid) is neither heavy nor light because heaviness and lightness are principles of ascending and descending rectilinear motion. If the determiner had such a principle, it would allow rectilinear motion, which would require a determined direction before it, leading to a contradiction. This proof is specific to the determiner. Then a general proof for all spheres is given: they are observed to move circularly, so they have a principle of circular inclination, not rectilinear, due to incompatibility. An objection is raised: the incompatibility may be denied because the two inclinations can coexist, producing a composite motion like rolling of a sphere or a wheel moving both rectilinearly and circularly. The response is that circular motion does not necessitate orientation toward a direction, so it does not conflict. If incompatibility is granted, it does not imply incompatibility between the principles themselves. The commentary clarifies that conventional circular motion does not involve exiting the place, so it does not require orientation. It also explains that the stone thrown upward does not have a natural rectilinear principle because nature cannot necessitate contradictory motions; hence, there is no natural or constrained rectilinear motion in the spheres.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The rolling motion of a wheel shows that rectilinear and circular motions can be combined naturally, so why can't spheres have both?
Correction: The text distinguishes between conventional circular motion (no change of place) and the rolling case. The spheres' motion is purely circular without directional orientation, so composite motion does not apply.

Key Takeaway
The celestial spheres have only circular motion because any rectilinear inclination would require a predetermined direction, which is impossible for the first sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Why Simplicity Prevents Circular Motion
Author refutes that simplicity causes circular motion; concludes a free chooser is needed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the simplicity of a celestial body cannot cause circular motion and how this argument leads to affirming a free chooser (mukhtar) rather than a necessary agent.

Core Idea
Simplicity and circular motion are incompatible; only a free chooser can explain the required differentiation.

Some philosophers argued that because celestial spheres are simple, they naturally move in circles. The author disagrees and shows that simplicity actually rules out circular motion. By analyzing what circular motion would require—specific poles, diverse circles, and differentiation among identical parts—he demonstrates that such motion cannot arise from a necessary agent. The only explanation is a free chooser who specifies without need for a preponderating cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author builds the argument: first a simple impossibility, then a more detailed geometrical argument, then the theological conclusion.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two reasons against simplicity causing circular motion
The author gives two main reasons: (1) if a simple body moves circularly, it would either move to all sides at once (impossible) or preferentially (requires a preponderator). (2) Circular motion requires specific poles and circles, but all points in a simple body are identical, so any assignment would be arbitrary (preponderance without a preponderator).
Both reasons rely on the principle of 'preponderance without a preponderator' being impossible.
3
Understand why a necessary agent cannot be the cause
The author argues that a necessary agent (موجب بالذات) cannot specify among equals without a preponderating cause. Since the celestial spheres are simple and all parts are equal, a necessary agent would treat them equally and could not produce the required differentiation.
4
See the conclusion: a free chooser is required
Because specification among identical parts is needed, the cause must be a free chooser (مختار) who acts by mere will without needing a preponderating cause. The author then recommends admitting this from the start to avoid many philosophical burdens.
This is a key argument for divine free will (irādah) in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثبوت البساطة في الكل قلنا : هي لا تقتضي الحركة بالاستدارة، بل تقتضي عدمها لأن البسيط إذا تحرك كذلك ) فإما أن يتحرك إلى جميع الجهات ) أي الجوانب دفعة واحدة ( وأنه محال أو إلى بعضها دون بعض ( وإنه ترجيح بلا مرجح) كما أن سكونه كذلك عندكم ، ( وأيضاً ) إذا تحرك البسيط على الاستدارة ( فلا بد ) هناك ( من قطبين) معينين ( ساكنين و ) من (دوائر) مخصوصة متفاوتة جداً في الصغر والكبر ) ترسمها الأجزاء ) والنقط المفروضة فيما بينهما ( حولهما بحركات مختلفة ) اختلافاً عظيماً بالسرعة والبطء مع استواء جميع النقط ( المفروضة ( فيه ) أي في البسيط ) وصلاحيتها للقطبية والسكون ورسم الدائرة الصغيرة أو الكبيرة بالحركة البطيئة أو السريعة ( وأنه ترجيح بلا (مرجح كما لا يخفى على ذي بصيرة (فلا يمكن إسناد ذلك ) أي تعيين بعض النقط للقطبية وبعضها لرسم الدائرة (إلى) فاعل ) موجب بالذات لأنه لا تخصيص من الموجب (إلا) لمرجح معد للقابل) فينتقل الكلام إليه (و) أيضاً نسبته إلى جميع الأجزاء سواء ) فلا يتصور منه تخصيص وتعيين فيما بينها ( بل إلى مختار ) يفعل ما يشاء بمجرد إرادته من غير احتياج إلى كما داع مرجح . ( وإذا وجب الرجوع بالآخرة إلى فعل المختار فليعترفوا به أولاً، فإنه يخفف عنهم كثيرا من المؤونات ) التي تلزمهم لإثبات قواعدهم الحكمية خصوصاً في أحكام الأفلاك، فإن تلك المؤونات مبنية على كون الواجب موجباً بالذات، فإذا قيل : إنه مختار سقطت
If the simplicity of all spheres is established, we say: it does not imply circular motion; rather it implies its absence, because if a simple body moves thus, then either it moves to all directions at once (which is impossible) or to some without others (which is preponderance without a preponderator) just as its rest is likewise according to you. Also, if a simple body moves circularly, there must be two specific fixed poles and particular circles, very different in size and smallness, traced by the parts and assumed points between them around them with different motions, great difference in speed and slowness, while all assumed points in the simple body are equal and suitable for being poles and rest and tracing a small or large circle with slow or fast motion, and that is preponderance without a preponderator, as is clear to one of insight. So it is impossible to attribute that assignment of some points as poles and some as tracing circles to a necessary agent, because the necessary does not specify except for a preponderating cause that prepares the recipient; so the discussion shifts to that. Also, its relation to all parts is equal, so specification and assignment among them cannot be conceived from it. Rather, it must be attributed to a free chooser who does whatever He wills by His mere will without need for a preponderating cause. And if the final recourse is to the action of the free chooser, let them admit this from the beginning, for it will relieve them of many burdens that they bear to establish their philosophical principles, especially regarding the judgments of the celestial spheres, for those burdens are built upon the Necessary Being being necessary in essence; if it is said that He is a free chooser, they fall away.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the idea that simplicity of celestial spheres implies circular motion. The author argues that simplicity would actually preclude circular motion because a simple body moving circularly would either move to all sides at once (impossible) or to some sides preferentially (which requires a differentiator). Furthermore, circular motion requires specific poles and circles, and if all points in the simple body are identical, any assignment of some points as poles and others as tracing circles would be arbitrary. This cannot be attributed to a necessary agent (موجب بالذات) because the necessary agent does not specify without a preponderating cause. Therefore, such specification must come from a free chooser (مختار). The author concludes that if one must ultimately resort to a free chooser, it is easier to admit this from the start, as it removes the burden of proving many philosophical principles about the celestial spheres.

مختار

Correction: The author shows that simplicity actually rules out circular motion because any circular motion of a simple body would require an arbitrary differentiation among identical parts, which is impossible without a free chooser.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Celestial Sphere and Its Daily Motion
Defines the daily motion and the encompassing celestial sphere with its poles and equator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the celestial sphere, its daily motion, and the significance of its poles and equinoctial circle.

Core Idea
The daily motion of the sky is the foundation of all apparent celestial movements.

In this passage, the author defines the encompassing celestial sphere and its most evident motion — the daily rotation. He explains why it is called the 'first motion' and identifies its stationary poles and the equinoctial circle, which is the celestial equator. This sets the stage for understanding the structure of the cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the English translation. Note the key terms: الفلك الأعظم, الحركة الأولى, قطبا العالم, معدل النهار.
The Arabic text is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the main components
The passage defines three things: the sphere itself, its motion, and its parts (poles and zone). List them in your own words.
The sphere encompasses all bodies; the motion is daily and swift.
3
Understand the rationale for 'first motion'
Why is the daily motion called the first? Because it is the most apparent and observed first, causing day/night and star risings/settings.
It is not hidden even from animals.
4
Connect poles and equinoctial to the sphere
Every rotating sphere must have stationary poles and a fastest zone. The equinoctial is the zone of fastest motion.
The poles are the poles of the world.
5
Reflect on the Earth's equator correspondence
The equinoctial circle in the sky corresponds to the Earth's equator, as mentioned in the text.
This will be detailed later in the investigations of the earth.
