⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وهو الفلك الأعظم) المحيط بجميع الأجسام لتحديده الجهات ( وحركته ( السريعة اليومية ( تسمى الحركة الأولى ) فإنها تشاهد أولاً من حركات الأفلاك لأنها أظهرها إذ بها الليل والنهار وطلوع الكواكب وغروبها، ولذلك لا تخفى على الحيوانات وكل كرة تحركت في مكانها على الاستدارة فلا بد لها من قطبين ساكنين ومن منطقة يكون حركتها أسرع فلذلك قال : ) وقطباها ) أي قطبا هذه الحركة أو الكرة ( قطبا العالم لأن العالم الجسماني هو المحدد، وما في ضمنه ( ومنطقته ( أعني أعظم دائرة تفرض في منتصف القطبين بحيث يتساوى بعدها عنهما تسمى معدل النهار ) لسبب ستقف عليه ) في مباحث الأرض (وهي) أي المنطقة المسماة بالمعدل ( حيث ) يكون ( لجميع الكواكب فيه طلوع و غروب ) ولا يكون هناك شيء منها أبدى الظهور ولا أبدى الخفاء ( تكون ملازمة لسمت الرأس مارة به وهو دويرة تامة من الأرض تسمى خط الاستواء كما ستعرفه
(And it is the greatest sphere) encompassing all bodies due to its determination of directions (and its (swift daily motion (is called the first motion) for it is observed first among the motions of the spheres because it is the most apparent, since through it occur night and day, rising and setting of the stars, and therefore it is not hidden from animals. And every sphere that moves in its place circularly must have two stationary poles and a zone where its motion is fastest. Therefore he said: And its two poles), i.e., the two poles of this motion or sphere (are the poles of the world, because the corporeal world is the encompassing one and what is within it, and its zone), i.e., the greatest circle assumed at the midpoint of the two poles such that its distance from them is equal, is called the equinoctial (for a reason you will learn) in the investigations of the earth. (And it), i.e., the zone called the equinoctial, (is where) for all stars there is rising and setting, and none of them is ever visible or ever hidden. (It is attached to the zenith passing through it, and it is a complete circle on the earth called the equator as you will know.)
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage defines the celestial sphere (al-falak al-a‘zam) and its daily motion. It begins by identifying the encompassing sphere that determines directions. Its swift daily motion is called the 'first motion' because it is the most evident, producing day and night and the apparent motion of stars. Then it explains that any sphere rotating must have two stationary poles and a zone of fastest motion. The poles of this motion are the poles of the world, since the corporeal world is the encompassing sphere and its contents. The zone is the greatest circle midway between the poles, called the equinoctial (mu‘addil al-nahār). This circle is characterized by all stars rising and setting (none are circumpolar). It passes through the zenith of a place on Earth and corresponds to a complete circle on Earth called the equator.

Checkpoint
Why is the daily motion called the 'first motion'?

B) Because it is the most apparent and first observed
C) Because it causes the seasons
D) Because it is the motion of the fixed stars

Key Takeaway
The daily motion of the celestial sphere defines the fundamental framework for understanding celestial phenomena.

The Sun's Inclination and Proper Motion
Describes the sun's oscillation and deduces its path is inclined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sun's path is inclined to the celestial equator and how its proper motion is inferred from observations.

Core Idea
The sun's north-south swing proves its orbit is tilted relative to the equinoctial.

The sun does not remain fixed on the celestial equator; over the year it wanders north and south. This observation leads astronomers to conclude that the sun's annual path is not the equator but an inclined circle. Additionally, the fact that the sun overtakes fixed stars shows it has a proper motion of its own.

Guided Path
1
Observe the sun's oscillation
Read the description of the sun's movement north and south of the zenith at the equator. Notice it oscillates between two extremes.
2
Identify the proof of inclination
The text argues that if the sun's path were in the plane of the equinoctial, there would be no north-south movement; therefore its path must be inclined.
This is a key logical inference.
3
Examine the proper motion evidence
The sun leaves fixed stars behind to the west, indicating it has a faster eastward motion than the stars.

(Unlike the sun, for it does not adhere to the zenith at the equator, but rather (inclines there sometimes northward, moving away from the zenith in those places, little by little to an extreme, then returns from that extreme, approaching it little by little until it is at the zenith, then inclines southward likewise, i.e., moving away from the zenith to an extreme equal to the first extreme, then returns from it approaching little by little until it reaches the zenith. Commentary
This passage explains the sun's motion relative to the celestial equator. Unlike the fixed stars, the sun does not always stay on the equator but oscillates north and south within a certain range. This oscillation proves that the sun's path (its orbit) is inclined to the equinoctial. Then the text uses the fact that the sun overtakes fixed stars to show it has a proper motion from west to east. A circle parallel to the sun's true path is imagined in the greatest sphere; this circle, as if the sun's path expanded, is called the ecliptic (minṭaqat al-burūj). It passes through the middles of the zodiacal constellations and is also the zone of the second motion (of the eighth sphere). Finally, it notes that the ecliptic and the equinoctial intersect at two opposite points (the equinoxes), as they are great circles.

The Ecliptic and Its Relation to the Equinoctial
Defines the ecliptic and describes its properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition and significance of the ecliptic circle.

Core Idea
The ecliptic is a conceptual great circle representing the sun's inclined annual path.

Having established that the sun's path is inclined, astronomers conceive a circle in the heavens that corresponds to this path. This circle, called the ecliptic, cuts the celestial equator at two points and is central to understanding planetary motion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the parallel circle
Read how a circle parallel to the sun's path is assumed in the greatest sphere, cutting through all beneath it.
2
Learn the names of this circle
The circle is called the ecliptic (because it passes through the middles of constellations), also the sphere of the constellations, and the zone of the second motion.
Apply technical terms.
3
Observe the intersection with the equinoctial
The ecliptic and equinoctial are both great circles, so they intersect at two opposite points (the equinoxes).

The Slow Motion of Fixed Stars: Precession Estimates and the Meaning of 'Fixed'
Al-Jurjani reports multiple estimates for the precession period and explains the name 'fixed'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different historical estimates for the precession period of the fixed stars and why they are called 'fixed' despite their motion.

Core Idea
The 'fixed' stars move slowly; their name reflects their imperceptible motion.

Al-Jurjani presents the various claims about the precession period of the fixed stars, ranging from 30,000 to 25,200 years, based on different observations. He then explains that these stars are called 'fixed' not because they are stationary, but because their motion is so slow that it was only discovered through careful long-term observation. This passage illustrates how early Islamic astronomers engaged critically with inherited and new observational data.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the estimates
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the different numbers given for the precession period and the sources cited.
There are four distinct estimates; notice which is attributed to Ptolemy and which to the Maragha observatory.
2
Identify the basis for each estimate
For each period, identify the observational basis: Ptolemy's 1 degree per century, later scholars' 1 degree per 66 years, and the Maragha group's 1 degree per 70 years.
The first estimate (30,000 years) is said to have no basis among astronomers.
3
Reflect on the name 'fixed'
Consider why the stars are called 'fixed' (thawābit) despite having a motion. The passage explains it is due to the extreme slowness of that motion.
This explains why ancient astronomers thought there were only eight spheres.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث: في فلك الثوابت. قد زعموا أن لها أي للثوابت مع كونها متحركة بالحركة اليومية تبعاً لفلك الأفلاك حركة خاصة بها بطيئة جدا وإنها تتم الدورة في ثلاثين ألف سنة. هذا قول قد اشتهر فيما بين العامة ولا أصل له عند أصحاب الأرصاد. وقيل: إنها تتم الدورة في ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة بناء على أن بطليموس وجد بالرصد أنها تقطع في كل مائة سنة جزءاً واحداً. وقيل: تتم الدورة في ثلاثة وعشرين ألف سنة وسبعمائة وستين سنة بناء على ما وجده المتأخرون من تقطع درجة واحدة في كل ست وستين سنة. وقيل: تتمها في خمسة وعشرين ألف سنة ومائتي سنة بناء على أن جماعة من محققي المتأخرين وجدوها تقطع جزءاً واحداً في كل سبعين سنة. وهذا هو الموافق للرصد الجديد الذي بمراغة. وإنما حكموا بإتمام الدورة فيما ذكر من المدد إذ قد أحس منها بحركة بطيئة بالرصد وجوه مختلفة كما عرفتها. واعتقادهم أنها تتم الدورة لدوامها على زعمهم فقدروا بالحساب تمام الدور في هذه المدة المختلفة فيها كما لخصناه. وإنما سميت ما عدا السبعة السيارة من الكواكب بالثوابت إما لبطء حركتها فلا نحس إلا بتدقيق النظر في أحوالها المعلومة بأرصاد بينها مدد طويلة. ولذلك اختفت على الأوائل حتى زعموا أن الأفلاك ثمانية وأن الحركة اليومية لكرة الثوابت.
The third purpose: On the sphere of the fixed stars. They claimed that they—i.e., the fixed stars—despite being moved by the daily motion following the sphere of spheres, have a motion particular to them, very slow, and that it completes a revolution in thirty thousand years. This is a famous saying among the common people, but it has no basis with the masters of observations. And it is said that it completes a revolution in thirty-six thousand years, based on the fact that Ptolemy found by observation that it cuts one degree every hundred years. And it is said that it completes a revolution in twenty-three thousand seven hundred and sixty years, based on what the later scholars found: that it cuts one degree every sixty-six years. And it is said that it completes it in twenty-five thousand two hundred years, based on that a group of later research scholars found it cutting one degree every seventy years. And this is in agreement with the new observation at Maragha. And they only ruled that the revolution is completed in the mentioned periods because they sensed from it a slow motion by observation in various ways, as you have known. And their belief that it completes the revolution due to its permanence, according to their claim, so they estimated by calculation the completion of the revolution in this disputed period as we have summarized. And why they called—apart from the seven moving planets—the stars 'fixed' (thawābit): either due to the slowness of their motion, so that we do not perceive it except by careful examination of their states known through observations between which long periods intervene. And for that reason they were hidden from the ancients until they claimed that the spheres are eight and that the daily motion belongs to the sphere of the fixed stars.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a report and analysis of the different scholarly opinions on the period of the precession of the fixed stars (the slow eastward motion of the equinoxes). The author begins by stating the claim that the fixed stars have a very slow motion of their own, despite participating in the daily motion. He lists several estimates: 30,000 years (popular but baseless), 36,000 years (based on Ptolemy's observation of 1 degree per century), 23,760 years (based on later scholars' 1 degree per 66 years), and 25,200 years (based on a group of later research scholars finding 1 degree per 70 years). The last figure is said to agree with the new observations at Maragha (the Maragha observatory). The author explains that these estimates arise from different observational methods and calculations over long periods. Then he explains why these stars are called 'fixed' (thawābit): because their motion is so slow that it is imperceptible without careful long-term observation. Because of this imperceptibility, the ancients thought there were only eight spheres and attributed the daily motion to the sphere of the fixed stars.

Key Terms
فلك الثوابت
حركة بطيئة
الدورة
بطليموس
الرصد الجديد
مراغة
ثوابت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fixed stars are completely stationary and do not move at all.
Correction: The fixed stars actually have a very slow motion (precession) that is only noticeable over long periods. The name 'fixed' refers to the difficulty of perceiving this motion, not absolute stillness.

Checkpoint
Which estimate for the precession period is said to agree with the new observation at Maragha?
A) 30,000 years
B) 36,000 years
C) 23,760 years
D) 25,200 years

Key Takeaway
The fixed stars have a slow motion of precession, with different historical estimates, and are called 'fixed' due to the difficulty of perceiving that motion.

?

Proving the Sun's Orbit is Eccentric or Epicyclic
Proves from observational data that the sun's orbit must be eccentric or epicyclic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how observational data from the equinoxes proves that the sun's orbit cannot be a simple concentric sphere and must involve eccentricity or an epicycle.

Core Idea
Observational evidence forces the sun's orbit to be eccentric or epicyclic.

How do we know the sun's orbit around Earth isn't a perfect circle? Medieval astronomers noticed that the time from the vernal equinox to the autumnal equinox is not equal to the time from the autumnal to the vernal. This difference in the lengths of the two halves of the year implies the sun moves at different speeds during the year. Since a perfectly centered orbit would produce uniform speed, the sun's path must be slightly off-center (eccentric) or involve a small epicycle.

Guided Path
1
Two possible orbital models
Learn about the two astronomical models that can account for varying distance and speed: an eccentric sphere (center not at Earth) or an epicycle (small circle carried by a concentric sphere).
These were standard in Ptolemaic astronomy.
2
The conditional argument
Understand the logical structure: if the sun were on a perfectly centered sphere, its distance from Earth would be constant, leading to uniform speed. So if we observe non-uniform speed, the orbit cannot be concentric.
This is a classic modus tollens argument.
3
Observational refutation
Read the observational evidence: the time from vernal to autumnal equinox is longer than half a year, and from autumnal to vernal is shorter. This proves the sun's speed is not uniform, so the orbit must be eccentric or epicyclic.
The inequality of the two halves is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي إما على فلك شامل للأرض ( مركزه خارج عن مركز العالم ) أو على ( فلك ( تدوير يحمله فلك موافق المركز وإلا ) أي وإن لم تكن الشمس على أحد الفلكين المذكورين ( لم تختلف بعداً وقربا ) بالنسبة إلى مركز العالم وما يليه من وجه الأرض (فلا تختلف سرعةً وبطأ كما علمت والتالي باطل بالرصد ) إذ قد وجدوا به أن الزمان المتخلل بين حلول الشمس الاعتدال الربيعي ثم الخريفي وهو نصف من فلك البروج أكثر من نصف السنة والمتخلل بين حلولها الخريفي ثم الربيعي وهو النصف الباقي منه أقل من نصف السنة فلا محالة تكون الشمس في النصف الأول أبطأ منها في النصف الثاني.
And it [the sun] is either on a sphere encompassing the earth (whose center is outside the center of the world) or on an epicycle carried by a concentric sphere. Otherwise (i.e., if the sun is not on one of the two mentioned spheres) it would not differ in distance and proximity relative to the center of the world and the surface of the earth, so it would not differ in speed and slowness as you have learned. And the consequent is false by observation, for they have found by it that the time between the sun's arrival at the vernal equinox and then the autumnal equinox, which is half of the zodiac circle, is more than half a year, and the time between its arrival at the autumnal and then the vernal, which is the remaining half, is less than half a year. So necessarily the sun is slower in the first half and faster in the second half.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from astronomical observation. It begins by stating that the sun must be either on an eccentric sphere (whose center is not Earth's center) or on an epicycle carried by a concentric sphere. The reasoning is conditional: if the sun were on a perfectly centered sphere, its distance from Earth would be constant, and thus its speed would be uniform. However, observation shows the opposite: the time between the vernal and autumnal equinoxes (spring+summer) is longer than half a year, while the time between the autumnal and vernal equinoxes (autumn+winter) is shorter. This means the sun moves slower in the first half (northern hemisphere) and faster in the second half (southern). Since a concentric orbit would not produce such variation, the sun's orbit must be either eccentric or epicyclic. The proof is a classic argument from empirical data in Ptolemaic astronomy.

Key Terms
فلك شامل للأرض (concentric sphere)
خارج المركز (eccentric)
تدوير (epicycle)
رصد (observation)
اعتدال ربيعي (vernal equinox)
اعتدال خريفي (autumnal equinox)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the sun's speed variation is due to its own internal motion or changes in its intrinsic speed.
Correction: The passage shows that the variation is a necessary geometric consequence of orbital distance change, not an intrinsic property of the sun.

Checkpoint
What does the observed inequality of the two halves of the year prove about the sun's orbit?
A) The sun moves on a perfect circle centered on Earth.
B) The sun's orbit must be eccentric or epicyclic.
C) The Earth moves around the sun.
D) The sun moves faster in winter.

Key Takeaway
The sun's variable speed, observed through unequal equinoctial intervals, proves that its orbital path is not a simple circle centered on Earth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have now seen how a careful observation of the equinoxes reveals the sun's true path. In your own life, what patterns have you been ignoring that might point to a deeper reality about your purpose or your relationship with your Creator? Will you commit to noticing one such sign this week?
?
The passage shows that what appears uniform (the sun's daily motion) is actually variable when measured precisely. What area of your faith or character do you assume is fine but might need closer inspection? What would it cost you to look honestly?
?
The proof forces a conclusion: the orbit cannot be simple. If you apply the same logical rigor to your own beliefs about God's existence or His attributes, do they hold up? What single piece of evidence from the world around you demands a conclusion you have been resisting?

The Four Elements and Their Direct Transformations
Defines the four elements' transformations and enumerates six direct ones with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of coming-to-be and passing-away for the four elements, and the six direct transformations that occur without a medium.

Core Idea
The four elements transform directly when they share one quality.

This passage opens the discussion of the four elements, explaining that they can shed and acquire forms, leading to twelve possible transformations. However, some transformations are direct, occurring without an intermediate element. The author focuses on the six direct ones, providing empirical examples from alchemy, evaporation, and blacksmithing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on definition and twelve transformations
First, read the Arabic and English definition of kawn and fasad, and the statement that each element can turn into the other three, giving twelve total. Notice the phrase 'لكن بعضها ينقلب إلى بعض آخر بلا وسط' which marks the transition to direct transformations.
2
Identify the three pairs of direct transformations
The passage lists three pairs: earth-water, water-air, air-fire. For each, note the shared quality (coldness, moisture, heat) and the example given. Write them down.
The examples include alchemical transformation, evaporation, and bellows.
3
Reflect on the logic
Why does sharing one quality enable direct transformation? The author implies that the elements are defined by two of the four primary qualities. If they share one, the other can change directly.
This is an Aristotelian framework.

المقصد الحادي عشر : العناصر الأربعة تقبل الاختلاط ] العناصر الأربعة تقبل الكون والفساد أي تخلع صورة ذلك العنصر وهو معنى الفساد وتلبس صورة عنصر آخر وهو معنى الكون فينقلب كل من الأربعة إلى الآخر الذي هو أحد الثلاثة الباقية فتكون الانقلابات اثنتي عشرة لكن بعضها ينقلب إلى بعض آخر بلا وسط وهو كل عنصر يشارك عنصر آخر في كيفية واحدة من كيفيتيه اللتين هما من الكيفيات الأربع ويخالفه في كيفية أخرى منهما فينقلب الأرض والماء كل منهما إلى الآخر ابتداء لاشتراكهما في البرد) . وإن اختلفا في اليبوسة (وذلك كما يجعل بعض أهل الحيل من طلاب الإكسير الأحجار مياهاً سيالة فإنهم يتخذون مياهاً حارة ويجعلون فيها أجساداً صلبة حجرية حتى تصير جارية وينقلب الماء في بعض المواضع حجراً صلباً كعين سیهكوه وهي قريبة من بلدة مراغة وماؤها ينقلب حجراً مرمراً، وعين غيره من المواضع وكذلك الماء والهواء ينقلب كل منهما إلى الآخر بلا وسط لاشتراكهما في الرطوبة وإن كانا متخالفين في الحرارة كما يصير الماء هواء بالتسخين وهو النشف في الثياب المبلولة المطروحة في الشمس و كما يصير الهواء ماء بالتبريد كما في ظاهر كوز لا مسام له يوضع في الجمد فإنه يحدث على ظاهره حيث لا يلاقيه الجمد قطرات من الماء وكظاهر الطاس يكب على الجمد مع عدم الملاقاة بينهما فإنه تركب قطرات منه وليس ذلك لأن الماء ينتقل إليه بالرشح لأنه لا يصعد بالطبع وإذ لو كان كذلك كان باطن الطاس أولى به من ظاهره . وأيضاً الترشح على سبيل التصاعد أنسب بالماء الحار وكذلك النار والهواء ينقلب كل منهما إلى الآخر بلا وسط لاشتراكهما في الحرارة وإن اختلفا في اليبوسة كما يصير الهواء ناراً في كير الحدادين بالإلحاح في النفخ مع سد المنافذ ثم تنطفئ النار فتصير هواء فهذه ست انقلابات بلا وسط بين المتشاركين في كيفية واحدة كيفيتهما وبعضها ينقلب إلى بعض آخر بواسطة وهو حيث يختلفان في الكيفيتين معاً كالماء والنار والهواء والأرض فإنه لا ينقلب الماء ناراً ابتداء لشدة تخالفهما . نعم قد ينقلب هواء ثم ناراً بأن ينقلب ذلك الهواء إلى النار وعليه فقس انقلاب النار ماء وانقلاب الهواء أرضاً وعكسه وأنت خبير بأن ما ذكروه يقتضي أن تنقلب كل واحدة من الأرض والنار إلى الأخرى بلا واسطة لاشتراكهما في اليبوسة والمشهور أنه بواسطتين فالأولى أن يقال : إن كان العنصران متجاورين كان الانقلاب بغير وسط وإن كان بينهما عنصر ثالث كان بواسطة واحدة وإن توسط بينهما اثنان فلا بد من واسطتين وهذا كله يدل على أن هيولى العناصر الأربعة واحدة مشتركة بينها وقابلة لجميع الصور العنصرية وإنما يعدها للصور المختلفة التي هي النارية والهوائية والمائية والأرضية . والكيفيات الأربع المتنافية ما عرض لها من القرب والبعد بالنسبة إلى الفلك وكل ما كان أقرب إليه كان أسخن وألطف . وكلما كان أبعد كان أبرد وأكثف وقد تكلمنا على مثله مراراً فلا نعيده . أي يمكن أن يقال : إن اختصاص بعض من الهيولى المشتركة بالقرب وبعضها بالبعد يحتاج إلى سبب من خارج فلا بد من الرجوع إلى المختار على أنا لا نسلم تركب الأجسام من الهيولى والصورة، ولا نسلم الانقلاب بين العناصر وما ذكروه من الأمثلة الدالة عليه يتطرق إليها احتمالات كثيرة .
The Eleventh Intention: The Four Elements Accept Mixing. The four elements accept coming-to-be and passing-away, i.e., they shed the form of that element (which is the meaning of passing-away) and put on the form of another element (which is the meaning of coming-to-be). Thus each of the four turns into the other which is one of the remaining three, so the transformations become twelve. But some of them turn into another without a medium, and that is every element that shares with another element in one of its two qualities (which are among the four qualities) and differs from it in the other of the two qualities. So earth and water each turn into the other initially due to their sharing in coldness, even though they differ in dryness. That is as some people of tricks (alchemy seekers) make stones into flowing water: they take hot water and put solid stony bodies in it until they become flowing. And water in some places turns into solid stone, like the spring of Siah Koh near Maragha, whose water turns into marble stone, and another spring elsewhere. Likewise water and air each turn into the other without a medium due to their sharing in moisture, though they differ in heat. As water becomes air by heating, which is evaporation, in wet clothes placed in the sun; and as air becomes water by cooling, as on the outside of a porous jar placed in ice: water drops form on its surface where the ice does not touch it. And like the outside of a basin placed over ice without contact: drops form on it. That is not because water transfers to it by seepage, because it does not rise naturally; otherwise the inside of the basin would be more likely than its outside. Also seepage upward is more appropriate for hot water. Likewise fire and air each turn into the other without a medium due to their sharing in heat, though they differ in dryness. As air becomes fire in the blacksmith's bellows by persistent blowing with blocked vents; then the fire goes out and becomes air. So these are six transformations without a medium between partners in one of their two qualities. And some of them turn into another by means of a medium, which is when they differ in both qualities together, like water and fire, air and earth. For water does not turn into fire initially due to their extreme opposition. Rather, it may turn into air then into fire, by that air turning into fire. Likewise, analogize the transformation of fire into water and air into earth and vice versa. And you know that what they have said implies that each of earth and fire turns into the other without a medium due to their sharing in dryness, but the widely held view is that it is by two media. So it is better to say: If the two elements are adjacent, the transformation is without a medium; if between them is a third element, it is by one medium; if two elements mediate, then two media are necessary. All this indicates that the matter (hayula) of the four elements is one, common among them, and receptive to all elemental forms. The four opposing qualities vary according to nearness and distance relative to the celestial sphere: whatever is closer to it is hotter and lighter; whatever is farther is colder and denser. We have spoken about similar matters repeatedly, so we do not repeat it. That is, it can be said: The specification of some of the common matter to nearness and some to distance requires an external cause, so one must refer to the Free Agent (al-Mukhtar). But we do not accept the composition of bodies from matter and form, nor do we accept transformation among the elements. The examples they mention that indicate it are open to many probabilities.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Aristotelian theory of the four elements (earth, water, air, fire) and their transformations. The author first defines 'coming-to-be' (al-kawn) and 'passing-away' (al-fasad) in elemental terms: an element sheds its form and acquires another. He then explains that each element can transform into any other, giving twelve possible transformations (4 × 3). However, some transformations occur directly (without a medium) and some indirectly (via one or two intermediates). The direct ones occur when two elements share one of the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness). For example, earth and water both share coldness, so they can transform directly; this is illustrated by alchemists turning stone to water and by natural springs that petrify. Similarly, water and air share moisture, so water evaporates into air and air condenses into water; examples include evaporation of wet clothes and formation of water droplets on a jar in ice. Air and fire share heat, so air can become fire in a blacksmith's bellows and fire can become air when extinguished. These account for six direct transformations. The indirect ones occur when elements share no quality, e.g., water and fire (opposite in heat and moisture/ dryness). Such transformations require an intermediate element: water becomes air first, then fire. Similarly for other pairs. The author notes a discrepancy: earth and fire share dryness, so they should transform directly, but the common opinion says two media are needed. He reconciles by saying if the elements are adjacent (in the order earth, water, air, fire), transformation can be direct; otherwise media are added. From all this, he concludes that the underlying matter (hayula) of the elements is one, and the differences in qualities arise from proximity to the celestial sphere (the heavens). This then requires an external cause to assign different distances, leading to the necessity of a Free Agent (God). However, the author (the commentator) distances himself from this entire theory, stating that he does not accept the composition of bodies from matter and form, nor the transformation between elements, and that the examples given are open to other interpretations. Thus the passage presents the Aristotelian view, uses it to argue for a Creator, but then rejects the foundation.

Key Terms

فساد
عناصر أربعة
كيفيات أربع

Indirect Transformations and the Argument for a Creator
Discusses indirect transformations, concludes on common matter, argues for a Free Agent, and then rejects the theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how indirect transformations require media, the conclusion that the four elements share a common matter, and how this leads to affirming a Free Agent, followed by the commentator's rejection of the whole theory.

Core Idea
The need for an external cause to arrange elemental distances points to a Creator, but the theory itself is rejected.

The passage continues from the direct transformations to those requiring one or two intermediate elements. The author then uses this to argue that the four elements share one underlying matter (hayula) that takes different forms due to distance from the celestial sphere. This distance requires an external cause, leading to the necessity of a Free Agent (al-Mukhtar). However, the commentator ends by distancing himself from the entire Aristotelian framework, stating he does not accept the composition of bodies from matter and form nor the transformation of elements, and that the examples are open to many interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on indirect transformations
Read the Arabic and English translation of the section starting from 'وبعضها ينقلب' to the end. Note the distinction between transformations requiring one medium (e.g., water to fire via air) and those requiring two media (e.g., earth to fire). Also note the author's reconciliation regarding earth-fire.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وأنت خبير' which signals the author's own comment.
2
Identify the conclusion about common matter (hayula)
The author states that all four elements share one hayula, and the differences in qualities are due to proximity to the celestial sphere. This is a key metaphysical point.
3
Understand the argument for a Free Agent
The specification of some matter to nearness and some to distance requires an external cause, which is the Free Agent (God). This is a typical kalam argument from contingency.
4
Read the commentator's rejection
The commentator explicitly says he does not accept the premises of this theory. Note the phrase 'على أنا لا نسلم' meaning 'But we do not accept'. This is crucial to avoid misunderstanding the author's stance.
The commentator is likely the author of the sharh, not the matn. The source chunk is from a sharh.

The Eleventh Intention: The Four Elements Accept Mixing. The four elements accept coming-to-be and passing-away, i.e., they shed the form of that element (which is the meaning of passing-away) and put on the form of another element (which is the meaning of coming-to-be). Thus each of the four turns into the other which is one of the remaining three, so the transformations become twelve. But some of them turn into another without a medium, and that is every element that shares with another element in one of its two qualities (which are among the four qualities) and differs from it in the other of the two qualities. So earth and water each turn into the other initially due to their sharing in coldness, even though they differ in dryness. That is as some people of tricks (alchemy seekers) make stones into flowing water: they take hot water and put solid stony bodies in it until they become flowing. And water in some places turns into solid stone, like the spring of Siah Koh near Maragha, whose water turns into marble stone, and another spring elsewhere. Likewise water and air each turn into the other without a medium due to their sharing in moisture, though they differ in heat. As water becomes air by heating, which is evaporation, in wet clothes placed in the sun; and as air becomes water by cooling, as on the outside of a porous jar placed in ice: water drops form on its surface where the ice does not touch it. And like the outside of a basin placed over ice without contact: drops form on it. That is not because water transfers to it by seepage, because it does not rise naturally; otherwise the inside of the basin would be more likely than its outside. Also seepage upward is more appropriate for hot water. Likewise fire and air each turn into the other without a medium due to their sharing in heat, though they differ in dryness. As air becomes fire in the blacksmith's bellows by persistent blowing with blocked vents; then the fire goes out and becomes air. So these are six transformations without a medium between partners in one of their two qualities. And some of them turn into another by means of a medium, which is when they differ in both qualities together, like water and fire, air and earth. For water does not turn into fire initially due to their extreme opposition. Rather, it may turn into air then into fire, by that air turning into fire. Likewise, analogize the transformation of fire into water and air into earth and vice versa. And you know that what they have said implies that each of earth and fire turns into the other without a medium due to their sharing in dryness, but the widely held view is that it is by two media. So it is better to say: If the two elements are adjacent, the transformation is without a medium; if between them is a third element, it is by one medium; if two elements mediate, then two media are necessary. All this indicates that the matter (hayula) of the four elements is one, common among them, and receptive to all elemental forms. The four opposing qualities vary according to nearness and distance relative to the celestial sphere: whatever is closer to it is hotter and lighter; whatever is farther is colder and denser. We have spoken about similar matters repeatedly, so we do not repeat it. That is, it can be said: The specification of some of the common matter to nearness and some to distance requires an external cause, so one must refer to the Free Agent (al-Mukhtar). But we do not accept the composition of bodies from matter and form, nor do we accept transformation among the elements. The examples they mention that indicate it are open to many probabilities.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Aristotelian theory of the four elements (earth, water, air, fire) and their transformations. The author first defines 'coming-to-be' (al-kawn) and 'passing-away' (al-fasad) in elemental terms: an element sheds its form and acquires another. He then explains that each element can transform into any other, giving twelve possible transformations (4 × 3). However, some transformations occur directly (without a medium) and some indirectly (via one or two intermediates). The direct ones occur when two elements share one of the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness). For example, earth and water both share coldness, so they can transform directly; this is illustrated by alchemists turning stone to water and by natural springs that petrify. Similarly, water and air share moisture, so water evaporates into air and air condenses into water; examples include evaporation of wet clothes and formation of water droplets on a jar in ice. Air and fire share heat, so air can become fire in a blacksmith's bellows and fire can become air when extinguished. These account for six direct transformations. The indirect ones occur when elements share no quality, e.g., water and fire (opposite in heat and moisture/ dryness). Such transformations require an intermediate element: water becomes air first, then fire. Similarly for other pairs. The author notes a discrepancy: earth and fire share dryness, so they should transform directly, but the common opinion says two media are needed. He reconciles by saying if the elements are adjacent (in the order earth, water, air, fire), transformation can be direct; otherwise media are added. From all this, he concludes that the underlying matter (hayula) of the elements is one, and the differences in qualities arise from proximity to the celestial sphere (the heavens). This then requires an external cause to assign different distances, leading to the necessity of a Free Agent (God). However, the author (the commentator) distances himself from this entire theory, stating that he does not accept the composition of bodies from matter and form, nor the transformation between elements, and that the examples given are open to other interpretations. Thus the passage presents the Aristotelian view, uses it to argue for a Creator, but then rejects the foundation.

Correction: The author presents the theory as a hypothetical argument for a Creator but ultimately rejects it, stating he does not accept the premises.

Moderate by Nature and the Eightfold Division of Temperaments
Definition and classification of moderate-by-nature temperaments with eightfold division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of 'moderate by nature' in temperament theory and how it leads to eight subdivisions for each main category.

Core Idea
Temperament is 'moderate by nature' when it suits the being's properties.

Sanusi now turns to temperaments that are not truly balanced. He distinguishes between those that are nevertheless 'moderate by nature'—where dominant qualities are appropriate for that being's character—and those that are not. He then shows how each of these two broad categories can be further subdivided into eight types based on four considerations: species, kind, individual, and organ, each viewed internally or externally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
Read the passage and find the two types: 'moderate by nature' and 'non-moderate.' Notice how they are defined by suitability.
2
Understand the eightfold division
The passage says each main type divides into eight sections. Trace the reasoning: four categories (species, kind, individual, organ) each considered in two ways (internal/external) gives 4x2=8.
The text cuts off; we only have the beginning of this explanation.
3
Apply to examples
Think about the lion and rabbit examples. How does 'moderate by nature' apply? Then consider how the eightfold division might work for different species.
This is conceptual; the full list is not provided.

ثم قالوا: وما ليس معتدلاً حقيقياً إن غلب عليه من الأجزاء في الكمية والكيفيات في الشدة ما ينبغي له ويليق به في خواصه وآثاره كالحرارة الغالبة في الأسد لشجاعته، والبرودة الغالبة في الأرنب لجبنه فهو المعتدل بحسب الطب وهو موجود. وإلا فغير المعتدل. وكل من القسمين ينقسم إلى ثمانية أقسام. فالمعتدل لأنه قد يعتبر بالنسبة إلى أمور أربعة: النوع والصنف والشخص والعضو ويعتبر كل من هذه الأربعة بالنسبة إلى الداخل تارة وإلى الخارج أخرى فكل نوع من المركبات
Then they said: And what is not truly balanced—if what dominates it in terms of parts in quantity and qualities in intensity is what is appropriate for it and befits it in its properties and effects, like heat dominating in the lion for its courage, and cold dominating in the rabbit for its cowardice—then it is the moderate by nature (al-muʿtadil bi-ḥasab al-ṭibb), and it exists. Otherwise it is the non-moderate. And each of the two divisions is divided into eight sections. For the moderate, it may be considered with respect to four matters: species, kind, individual, and organ, and each of these four is considered sometimes in relation to the internal and sometimes to the external. So every type of composite...
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage classifies temperaments that are not truly balanced. Two categories are introduced: (1) moderate by nature, where the dominant qualities are suitable for the being's properties (e.g., heat in the lion for courage), and (2) non-moderate, where the dominant qualities are unsuitable. Each of these two categories is further divided into eight sections. The division arises because temperament can be considered relative to four different categories: species, kind, individual, and organ. Moreover, each of these four can be considered in relation to the internal (the composite's interior) or the external (its apparent properties). Thus, 4 × 2 = 8 subdivisions for each main type. The text begins to mention 'every type of composite' but is cut off.

The Necessity of a Creator from Design in Nature
Presents a necessary proof for a wise, powerful Creator from design in animal generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how contemplating the wonders of animal generation necessarily leads to the knowledge of a Creator who is all-knowing, all-aware, wise, and powerful.

Core Idea
Contemplating design in nature yields necessary knowledge of a perfect Creator.

This passage presents a powerful argument from design. The author invites us to contemplate the wonders of plants and animals, especially the intricate processes in the womb. Without prejudice, such contemplation leads to the undeniable conclusion that these acts cannot be from unconscious forces but only from a perfectly knowledgeable and wise agent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the prerequisites for correct contemplation
The passage begins by describing the conditions for valid reflection: intelligence, fairness, and remaining on the natural disposition free from blind following. This sets the stage for the argument.
Without these prerequisites, one's insight may be blinded.
2
Recognize the impossibility of unconscious forces
The author argues that the intricate forms, shapes, and positions in the womb cannot be produced by simple or compound powers that are unconscious. They require a knowing agent.
The phrase 'powerless forces' refers to natural forces devoid of knowledge.
3
Appreciate the overwhelming evidence from known and unknown benefits
Over five thousand benefits of animal organs are recorded, and many more are unknown. This vast amount of wisdom cannot be accidental.
4
Grasp the necessary attributes derived
From the design, the contemplator necessarily knows that the agent must be all-knowing (alīm), all-aware (khabīr), wise (hakīm), and powerful (qadīr).
This knowledge is necessary, not speculative.
5
See the Quranic confirmation and the practical benefit
The Quran (3:6) is cited as evidence that this is necessary knowledge. Acknowledging the free agent frees one from philosophical contortions.

أسيراً في مطمورة (الوهم ) أي في سجنه بأن لا يغلب وهمه على عقله، (علم) ذلك المتأمل ) بالضرورة أنها أي تلك الأفعال العجيبة البالغة تلك الدرجة العالية ( لا يمكن أن تستند إلى قوى بسيطة) أو مركبة ( عديمة الشعور) بما يفرض صادراً عنها (سيما ما يحدث في الحيوانات ( من الصور والأشكال والتحطيطات المقدارية والأوضاع المتلائمة ( في الرحم وما يفاض فيه ( من الصور) النوعية ( والقوى ) التابعة لها ) على تلك المادة المتشابهة الأجزاء ) على الرأي الأصوب ) وما يراعى فيها ) أي في تلك الأمور الحادثة والمفاضة (من) حكم و ( مصالح قد تحيرت فيها الأوهام وعجزت عن إدراكها العقول و ( الأفهام، قد بلغ المدون منها ) أي من تلك الحكم والمصالح ) مما علم ( في الكتب التي دون فيها منافع أعضاء الحيوانات وأشكالها ومقاديرها وأوضاعها ( خمسة آلاف وما لا يعلم منها (أكثر) مما علم كما لا يخفى على ذي حدس كامل ( وعلم ) ذلك المتأمل أيضاً ( علما ضرورياً لا يشوبه ريبة ولا يحتمل النقيض بوجه ) من الوجوه ( أنها ) أي تلك الأفعال المذكورة لا تصدر إلا عن عليم كامل علمه ( خبير) ببواطن الأشياء وما يخفى منها، ( حكيم ) يتقن أفعاله مطابقة للمنافع التي يتصور ترتبها عليها، (قدير) على كل ما تعلقت به مشيئته بعد علمه المحيط ( كما نطق به الكتاب) الكريم (في عدة مواضع في معرض الاستدلال على عظمة الصانع وكماله، منها قوله تعالى : وهو الذي يصوركم في الأرحام كيف يشاء )، فدل إيراده في معرضه على أنه علم ضروري يستدل به على غيره . هذا هو الحق الذي لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه ) على أن في الاعتراف بالفاعل المختار ) وإسناد الأشياء إليه إبتداء كما مرت إليه الإشارة مرة بعد أخرى فائدة جليلة هي أن فيه لمندوحة عن كثير من أمثال ( هذه التمحلات التي يكذبها العقل الصريح ويأباها الذهن الصحيح ولا يقبلها طبع سليم ولا يذعن لها ذهن مستقيم ربنا لا تزغ قلوبنا بعد إذ هديتنا وهب من لدنك رحمة إنك أنت الوهاب) منك المبدأ وإليك المآب . )
Then we say in refuting the faculties, especially the formative power as they claim: Indeed, one who contemplates the wonders of the actions occurring in natural science—of diverse types of plants and animals of different natures, reaching in those wondrous actions the utmost degree of perfection and precision, and that contemplation is accompanied by intelligence and fairness, remaining upon the natural disposition of God which He created people upon—of intelligence and inclination to correctness—his insight will not be blinded by blind following of the people of passions, and he will not be a [؟]. And his saying: 'and all his entrails' meaning the things that are in his inside. And the word 'al-zakhīr' is with the dotted 'z' and unpointed 'ḥ', meaning diarrhea, and the 'baḥrāniyya' evacuations are those caused by intense heat. His saying: 'and did not blind' with kasra on the 'mīm' from 'ta'mīm', and 'his insight' is its object, and 'blind following' is its subject. And his saying: 'from the people of passions' is a circumstantial qualifier from the implicit pronoun in 'did not blind', so the circumstantial qualifier here is a restriction on the negated, not [؟]. And 'al-maṭmūra' is a pit, therefore he interpreted it as prison. And his saying: 'he knew' is the predicate of 'anna', and 'al-takhṭīṭāt' is narrated with unpointed 'ḥā' and two unpointed 'ṭā's from 'ḥaṭṭa al-raḥl' i.e. he alighted, so 'taḥṭīṭ' of a thing is placing it in its station and rank with respect to its measure, position, and shape. And it is also narrated with two dotted 'ẓā's, so 'taḥẓīẓ' of a thing means giving it its shares of measure, position, and shape. And his saying: 'according to the more correct opinion' means that the preferred view concerning semen is that it is homogeneous in parts, not heterogeneous; and upon this, the attribution of its stages to the free-acting, knowing, wise agent is more evident. [First observatory - third chapter - first section: the vegetative soul] A prisoner in the dungeon of illusion, i.e., in its prison, so that his illusion does not overcome his intellect. That contemplator knows necessarily that those wondrous actions reaching that high degree cannot be attributed to simple or compound powers lacking consciousness of what is supposed to issue from them, especially what occurs in animals—the forms, shapes, quantitative delineations, and harmonious positions in the womb and what is infused into it of specific forms and the powers subordinate to them upon that matter homogeneous in parts according to the more correct opinion—and what is observed in them of wisdom and benefits at which illusions are bewildered and intellects and minds are incapable of grasping. The recorded part of them has reached from what is known in the books that contain the benefits of animal organs, their shapes, measures, and positions five thousand, and what is not known of them is more than what is known, as is not hidden from one of perfect intuition. And that contemplator also knows with necessary knowledge, untainted by doubt and not admitting its opposite in any way, that those mentioned actions do not issue except from one all-knowing in His knowledge, aware of the inner aspects of things and what is hidden from them, wise who perfects His actions conforming to the benefits that are imagined to follow from them, powerful over everything to which His will attaches after His encompassing knowledge, as the noble Book has spoken in several places in the context of inferring the greatness and perfection of the Maker, among them His saying: And He is the one who forms you in the wombs as He wills. So its mention in that context indicates that it is necessary knowledge used to infer other than Him. This is the truth that falsehood cannot approach from before it or from behind it. And to acknowledge the free agent and to attribute things to Him initially, as has been hinted repeatedly, is a great benefit: it provides an escape from many such contortions that clear reason falsifies, sound mind rejects, healthy nature refuses, and upright intellect does not submit to. Our Lord, do not cause our hearts to deviate after You have guided us, and grant us mercy from Yourself; indeed You are the Bestower. From You is the beginning, and to You is the return.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents a powerful proof for the existence and attributes of God from the design in nature, particularly in animal generation. The author begins by stating that anyone who contemplates the wonders of natural actions—diverse plants and animals, all reaching perfection—and does so with fairness and not blinded by blind following, will realize the truth. The commentary clarifies some terms: 'zakhīr' means diarrhea, 'baḥrāniyya' evacuations are due to intense heat; 'blind' means not overcome by habit; 'dungeon of illusion' is a prison for the intellect. The main argument: such intricate actions—forms, shapes, quantitative delineations, harmonious positions in the womb, and the infusion of specific forms and powers into a homogeneous matter—cannot be attributed to simple or compound powers that are unconscious. The huge number of benefits (five thousand recorded) and the many more unknown show that the agent must be all-knowing (alīm), aware of inner things (khabīr), wise (ḥakīm) in perfectly fitting actions to benefits, and powerful (qadīr) over everything to which His will attaches. The Quranic verse Q. 3:6 is cited as evidence that this is necessary knowledge used to infer the Creator. The conclusion: this truth is inescapable, and acknowledging the free agent frees one from many implausible philosophical contortions. The passage ends with a prayer for guidance.

Proof from Design in Animal Creation to the Wise Creator
The author argues from design in animal creation to the necessary knowledge of a wise and powerful Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intricate design of animal bodies necessarily leads to the knowledge of a Creator who is all-knowing, wise, and powerful.

The design in animal bodies yields necessary knowledge of a wise Creator.

The author presents a powerful argument from design, focusing on the remarkable formation of animals in the womb. He asserts that this intricate and purposeful arrangement cannot arise from unconscious forces, but must come from a Being with perfect knowledge and wisdom. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أسيراً في مطمورة (الوهم) أي في سجنه بأن لا يغلب وهمه على عقله، (علم) ذلك المتأمل (بالضرورة أنها أي تلك الأفعال العجيبة البالغة تلك الدرجة العالية (لا يمكن أن تستند إلى قوى بسيطة) أو مركبة (عديمة الشعور) بما يفرض صادراً عنها (سيما ما يحدث في الحيوانات (من الصور والأشكال والتخطيطات المقدارية والأوضاع المتلائمة (في الرحم وما يفاض فيه (من الصور) النوعية (والقوى) التابعة لها (على تلك المادة المتشابهة الأجزاء) على الرأي الأصوب (وما يراعى فيها) أي في تلك الأمور الحادثة والمفاضة (من) حكم و (مصالح قد تحيرت فيها الأوهام وعجزت عن إدراكها العقول و (الأفهام، قد بلغ المدون منها) أي من تلك الحكم والمصالح (مما علم (في الكتب التي دون فيها منافع أعضاء الحيوانات وأشكالها ومقاديرها وأوضاعها (خمسة آلاف وما لا يعلم منها (أكثر) مما علم كما لا يخفى على ذي حدس كامل (وعلم) ذلك المتأمل أيضاً (علما ضرورياً لا يشوبه ريبة ولا يحتمل النقيض بوجه) من الوجوه (أنها) أي تلك الأفعال المذكورة لا تصدر إلا عن عليم كامل علمه (خبير) ببواطن الأشياء وما يخفى منها، (حكيم) يتقن أفعاله مطابقة للمنافع التي يتصور ترتبها عليها، (قدير) على كل ما تعلقت به مشيئته بعد علمه المحيط (كما نطق به الكتاب) الكريم (في عدة مواضع في معرض الاستدلال على عظمة الصانع وكماله، منها قوله تعالى: ﴿هُوَ الَّذِي يُصَوِّرُكُمْ فِي الْأَرْحَامِ كَيْفَ يَشَاءُ﴾ [آل عمران: ٦]، فدل إيراده في معرضه على أنه علم ضروري يستدل به على غيره. هذا هو الحق الذي لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه (على أن في الاعتراف بالفاعل المختار) وإسناد الأشياء إليه ابتداء كما مرت إليه الإشارة مرة بعد أخرى فائدة جليلة هي أن فيه مندوحة عن كثير من أمثال (هذه التمحلات التي يكذبها العقل الصريح ويأباها الذهن الصحيح ولا يقبلها طبع سليم ولا يذعن لها ذهن مستقيم ربنا لا تزغ قلوبنا بعد إذ هديتنا وهب من لدنك رحمة إنك أنت الوهاب ﴾ [آل عمران: ٨] منك المبدأ وإليك المآب.
A prisoner in the dungeon of illusion (al-wahm), that is, in its prison, so that his illusion does not overcome his intellect, (he knows) that meditator knows necessarily that those amazing actions reaching that high degree cannot be based on simple or composite powers devoid of consciousness from what is supposed to proceed from them, especially what occurs in animals of forms, shapes, quantitative delineations, and harmonious dispositions in the womb, and what is infused therein of specific forms and powers dependent on them into that matter similar in parts according to the most correct view, and what is observed in them, that is, in those things that occur and are infused, of wisdoms and benefits which have bewildered illusions and which intellects and understandings have been incapable of comprehending. The recorded thereof has reached, from what is known in the books in which the benefits of the organs of animals and their forms, amounts, and dispositions are compiled, five thousand, and what is not known thereof is more than what is known, as is not hidden from one with perfect intuition. And that meditator also knows with necessary knowledge, which no doubt contaminates and which admits of no opposite in any respect, that those mentioned actions proceed only from an all-knowing (ʿalīm) whose knowledge is perfect, an expert (khabīr) in the inner dimensions of things and what is hidden from them, a wise (ḥakīm) who perfects his acts in accordance with the benefits that are conceived to follow from them, and powerful (qadīr) over everything to which His will is attached after His encompassing knowledge, as the noble Book has declared in several places in the context of inference to the greatness and perfection of the Maker. Among them is His saying: 'He it is who forms you in the wombs as He wills' (Qur’ān 3:6). So its mention in that context indicates that it is necessary knowledge by which one infers to other matters. This is the truth which falsehood cannot approach from before it or from behind it. That in acknowledging the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) and attributing things to Him initially, as has been indicated repeatedly, there is a great benefit, namely that it provides an escape from many of these fallacious speculations which explicit reason falsifies, which sound intellect rejects, which a sound nature does not accept, and to which a straight mind does not submit. 'Our Lord, do not cause our hearts to deviate after You have guided us, and grant us from Yourself mercy. Indeed, You are the Bestower' (Qur’ān 3:8). From You is the beginning and to You is the return.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from design for the existence and attributes of God. It begins by describing the ideal contemplator as one who imprisons his imagination (wahm) under his intellect, preventing fancy from overwhelming reason. Such a contemplator necessarily knows that the wondrous actions observed in animals—especially the formation of organs in the womb with precise shapes, sizes, and harmonies—cannot originate from simple or composite unconscious powers. The text emphasizes the incredible number of recorded benefits (five thousand) in anatomical texts, implying that these far exceed what human intellect can fully grasp. This necessary knowledge leads to the conclusion that these actions must proceed from a being who is all-knowing (ʿalīm), expert (khabīr), wise (ḥakīm), and powerful (qadīr). The author cites Qur’ān 3:6 ('He who forms you in the wombs as He wills') as evidence that this knowledge is necessary and used in the Qur’an as a proof. He then underscores the value of affirming the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) and ascribing all things to Him, as this protects against baseless philosophical speculations that sound reason rejects. The passage ends with a prayer for steadfastness and a recognition that all beginnings and returns are from God.

عليم
خبير
حكيم
قدير
علم ضروري

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from design only gives probable knowledge.
Correction: The author explicitly states that this knowledge is necessary (ʿilm ḍarūrī), free from doubt and not admitting of an opposite.

Checkpoint
What does the argument from design conclude about the characteristics of the Creator?
A) He is powerful but not necessarily wise.
B) He is knowing, expert, wise, and powerful.
C) He is only the creator of humans, not animals.
D) He is unknown and unknowable.

Key Takeaway
The intricate design of animals necessarily points to a Creator with perfect knowledge, wisdom, and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the intricate design of your own body—your organs, their shapes and functions—do you feel the necessary knowledge of the Creator as described, or have you become numb to this reality? What would it take for this truth to move from mental acknowledgment to heartfelt certainty that changes how you live today?
?
The author says that accepting the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) and attributing all things to Him provides an escape from fallacious speculations. Are there intellectual doubts or philosophical questions you have been entertaining that, in reality, stem from not fully submitting your intellect to this necessary knowledge? Will you actively reject those speculations and embrace the truth that God alone is the Creator?
?
Recognizing that God forms you in the womb 'as He wills' (Qur'an 3:6), how does this reshape your understanding of your purpose and your life's events? What is one specific area of your life—your career, relationships, or struggles—where you need to stop attributing outcomes to mere chance or unconscious forces and instead see the hand of the All-Knowing, Wise Creator? Commit to a concrete action that reflects this trust.

The Second Division: Bodies Eternal in Essence and Attributes
Introduction to the second division and its proponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general doctrine of the second division – that bodies are eternal in essence and attributes – and who its proponents are.

Core Idea
Aristotle and his followers teach that all bodies are eternal in both essence and attributes.

The author begins by outlining the second major division among theories of bodies: the claim that bodies are eternal in their very selves and attributes. He attributes this view to Aristotle and his prominent later followers, al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā. This lesson introduces the core claim and its proponents before diving into the detailed sub-divisions.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic passage that states the second division and its key claim. Note the phrase 'قديمة بذواتها وصفاتها'.
This is the central thesis of the Aristotelians.
2
Identify the authorities
Look for the names of the philosophers who held this view: Aristotle, al-Fārābī, and Ibn Sīnā.
These are the key figures of the Peripatetic tradition.
3
Understand the initial division
Notice the transition to the detailed doctrine, which divides bodies into celestial and elemental.
This division is fundamental to their explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنها قديمة بذواتها وصفاتها وإليه ذهب أرسطو ومن تبعه من متأخري الفلاسفة كالفارابي وابن سينا، وتفصيل مذهبهم أنهم قالوا : الأجسام تنقسم كما علمت إلى فلكيات وعنصريات، أما الفلكيات فإنها قديمة بموادها وصورها الجسمية والنوعية ( وأعراضها ( المعينة من المقادير والأشكال وغيرها ) إلا الحركات والأوضاع المشخصة فإنها حادثة ) قطعاً ضرورة أن كل حركة شخصية مسبوقة بأخرى لا إلى نهاية وكذا الأوضاع المعينة التابعة لها، وأما مطلق الحركة والوضع فقديم أيضاً لأن مذهبهم أن الأفلاك متحركة مستمرة من الأزل إلى الأبد بلا سكون أصلاً . ( وأما العنصريات فقديمة بموادها وبصورها الجسمية (بنوعها وذلك لأن المادة لا تخلو عن الصورة الجسمية التي هي طبيعة واحدة نوعية لا تختلف إلا بأمور خارجة عن حقيقتها فيكون نوعها مستمر الوجود بتعاقب أفرادها أزلاً وأبداً ، ) وبصورها النوعية بجنسها ) وذلك لأن مادتها لا يجوز خلوها عن صورها النوعية بأسرها بل لا بد أن يكون معها واحدة منها لكن هذه الصور متشاركة في جنسها دون ماهيتها النوعية فيكون جنسها مستمر الوجود بتعاقب أنواعه (نعم) الصور المشخصة فيهما أي في الصورة الجسمية والنوعية ( والأعراض المختصة ( المعينة ( محدثة ولا امتناع في حدوث بعض الصور النوعية ) العنصرية كأن يكون مثلاً نوع الإنسان حادثاً غير مستمر الوجود تتعاقب أفراده الشخصية، إذ يجوز حصوله من عنصر آخر بطريق الكون والفساد ولا امتناع أيضاً عندهم في استمراره كذلك ولا في استمرار أنواع المركبات في ضمن أفرادها المتعاقبة بلا نهاية
The second: They are eternal in their essences and attributes, and to it went Aristotle and those who followed him among the later philosophers, like al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā. The detail of their doctrine is that they said: Bodies are divided, as you have learned, into celestial and elemental. As for celestial bodies, they are eternal in their matters, their corporeal and specific forms, and their determinate accidents – quantities, shapes, and others – except for the individual motions and positions, for they are originated, necessarily, because every individual motion is preceded by another ad infinitum, and likewise the determinate positions dependent on them. As for absolute motion and position, they are also eternal, because their doctrine is that the celestial spheres are in continuous motion from eternity to eternity without any rest at all. As for elemental bodies, they are eternal in their matters and in their corporeal forms by their type, because matter is never devoid of the corporeal form, which is a single nature of a type that does not differ except by matters external to its reality; so its type is continuously existent through the succession of its individuals eternally and forever; and in their specific forms by their genus, because their matter cannot be devoid of all their specific forms, but rather must have one of them with it. However, these forms share in their genus but not in their specific quiddity; so their genus is continuously existent through the succession of its types. Indeed, the individualizing forms in both – that is, in the corporeal and specific forms – and the specific determinate accidents are originated. There is no impossibility in the origination of some elemental specific forms, such as for example the human type being originated, not continuously existent, with its personal individuals succeeding one another, for it is possible that it arises from another element by way of generation and corruption. And there is also no impossibility in their view in its continuation thus, nor in the continuation of the types of compounds within their successive individuals ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents the second division of bodies – that they are eternal in both essence and attributes. The author attributes this view to Aristotle and his later followers, al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā. The exposition then provides a detailed breakdown of the philosophers' position by dividing bodies into celestial (falakiyyāt) and elemental (ʿunṣuriyyāt). For celestial bodies, they are eternal in matter, corporeal form, specific form, and determinate accidents (quantities, shapes, etc.) – except for individual motions (ḥarakāt shakhṣiyya) and positions (awḍāʿ), which are originated. The reason given is that each individual motion is preceded by another to infinity, so there cannot be a first motion. However, motion in the absolute sense (muṭlaq al-ḥaraka) and position (al-waḍʿ) are eternal because the celestial spheres move continuously from pre‑eternity to post‑eternity without ever ceasing. For elemental bodies, the philosophers hold them eternal in matter and in corporeal form by type (bi‑nawʿihā), because matter always has a corporeal form of a single type, and the type persists through successive individuals. They are also eternal in specific forms by genus (bi‑jinsihā), because matter must always have some specific form, and these specific forms share a genus that persists through successive types. However, the individualizing forms (al-ṣuwar al-mushakhkhiṣa) in both corporeal and specific forms, as well as the determinate accidents, are originated. The author adds that there is no contradiction in the view that some elemental specific forms (like the human type) may be originated and not continuously existent, as they could arise from another element via generation and corruption. Similarly, the continuation of such types or the types of compounds within successive individuals ad infinitum is possible in their doctrine. This detailed exposition clarifies the nuanced stance of the Peripatetic philosophers.

Key Terms
الثاني
قديمة بذواتها وصفاتها
أرسطو
الفارابي
ابن سينا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all philosophers who believe in eternal bodies deny any originated aspects.
Correction: The passage introduces a nuanced view: bodies are eternal in essence and attributes, but the detailed doctrine will show that certain properties like individual motions are originated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which philosopher is NOT explicitly mentioned as a follower of this view?
A) Aristotle
B) al-Fārābī
C) Ibn Sīnā
D) Plato

Key Takeaway
The second division holds that bodies are eternal in both essence and attributes, a view championed by Aristotle and his later followers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that Aristotle and his followers believed bodies are eternal. Do you ever find yourself living as if the world is self-sufficient, forgetting that it is constantly sustained by Allah? How can you actively remind yourself of your dependence on the Creator each day?
?
If you truly believed that the universe has always existed, how would that affect your gratitude for the blessing of existence? Contrast that with the Islamic view that every moment is a gift from Allah – which perspective compels you to be more thankful?
?
Consider the philosophers mentioned: they held a rational argument for eternity. Has your own trust in divine revelation ever been challenged by worldly philosophies? What steps can you take to strengthen your conviction in the Qur'anic account of creation?

Celestial Bodies: Eternal and Originated Aspects
Detailed analysis of celestial bodies: what is eternal and what is originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which aspects of celestial bodies are considered eternal and which are originated according to the Aristotelians.

Core Idea
Celestial bodies are eternal in matter, form, and accidents, but individual motions and positions are originated.

After the general division, the author explains the status of celestial bodies. He details that while their matter, corporeal form, specific form, and determinate accidents are eternal, their individual motions and positions are originated. This lesson unpacks that nuanced position and the reasoning behind the origination of individual motions.

Guided Path
1
Read the celestial bodies section
Read the passage that begins with 'أما الفلكيات' and ends with 'بلا سكون أصلاً'.
Pay attention to the list of eternal components.
2
Identify the eternal components
List what the philosophers say is eternal for celestial bodies: materials, corporeal and specific forms, determinate accidents like quantities and shapes.
These are considered unchanging in essence.
3
Spot the originated exception
Find the phrase 'إلا الحركات والأوضاع المشخصة' and understand why individual motions and positions are originated.
The reason given is that each individual motion is preceded by another ad infinitum.
4
Understand absolute vs individual
Note the distinction between absolute motion/position (eternal) and individual motions/positions (originated).
Absolute motion is continuous, but each specific instance is new.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وأما العنصريات فقديمة بموادها وبصورها الجسمية (بنوعها وذلك لأن المادة لا تخلو عن الصورة الجسمية التي هي طبيعة واحدة نوعية لا تختلف إلا بأمور خارجة عن حقيقتها فيكون نوعها مستمر الوجود بتعاقب أفرادها أزلاً وأبداً ، ) وبصورها النوعية بجنسها ) وذلك لأن مادتها لا يجوز خلوها عن صورها النوعية بأسرها بل لا بد أن يكون معها واحدة منها لكن هذه الصور متشاركة في جنسها دون ماهيتها النوعية فيكون جنسها مستمر الوجود بتعاقب أنواعه (نعم) الصور المشخصة فيهما أي في الصورة الجسمية والنوعية ( والأعراض المختصة ( المعينة ( محدثة ولا امتناع في حدوث بعض الصور النوعية ) العنصرية كأن يكون مثلاً نوع الإنسان حادثاً غير مستمر الوجود تتعاقب أفراده الشخصية، إذ يجوز حصوله من عنصر آخر بطريق الكون والفساد ولا امتناع أيضاً عندهم في استمراره كذلك ولا في استمرار أنواع المركبات في ضمن أفرادها المتعاقبة بلا نهاية
The second: They are eternal in their essences and attributes, and to it went Aristotle and those who followed him among the later philosophers, like al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā. The detail of their doctrine is that they said: Bodies are divided, as you have learned, into celestial and elemental. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you consider the events in your own life—your daily actions—as part of an eternal plan or as originated moments? How does this distinction affect your sense of responsibility for each action?
?

?
The celestial spheres are in continuous motion without rest. Reflect on your own life: are you constantly moving without rest? What does this passage teach you about the difference between the necessary motion of the heavens and the voluntary, originated actions you take? How can you align your actions with the eternal purpose given by Allah?
